Metal Shaders
GPU from zero: MSL, fragments, noise, blur, distortion, real Apple effects.

    Metal Shading Language (MSL) — Learning Roadmap

    Start here — the plain idea

    Think of this page as the map for a long road trip. You're not learning anything technical yet — you're just seeing the route, the stops, and the order to drive them in. Each lesson is one stop, and the stops are arranged so each one only assumes what you've already seen.

    The big picture: a shader is a tiny program that runs on the graphics chip (the GPU). The course starts with why that chip exists and how to think for it, then teaches the language, then walks through real visual effects you can copy. Don't try to memorize the table below — just know it's here when you want to find your place.

    A self-paced curriculum from zero to writing production-quality shaders for iOS.

    How to Use This Series

    Each file builds on the previous one. Read them in order. Every lesson includes: - Concepts — what you're learning and why - Code — complete, runnable shader code - Exercises — things to try yourself - Checkpoint — what you should understand before moving on

    • Basic Swift knowledge
    • Xcode installed
    • An iOS device or Simulator

    Recommended Tools

    • Xcode — write, compile, and debug Metal shaders
    • Metal Debugger (Xcode > GPU Frame Capture) — inspect shader execution
    • Shader Playground apps — for rapid iteration
    • Instruments > Metal System Trace — for performance analysis

    The foundation

    • Shader = a small program that runs on the GPU, once per pixel or vertex.
    • Curriculum order = each file assumes the ones before it — drive the stops in sequence.

    Next01 — GPU vs CPU: Why Shaders Exist →

    01 — GPU vs CPU: Why Shaders Exist

    Start here — the plain idea

    Imagine you have to paint a stadium full of seats one color. A CPU is like a handful of geniuses who can do anything but only work one seat at a time. A GPU is like ten thousand simple painters who all dab their one assigned seat at the same instant. For "do the same simple thing to a million pixels," the army of simple painters wins by a mile.

    A shader is just the instruction sheet you hand every painter. You write the rule for one pixel — "what color should you be?" — and the GPU runs your rule on every pixel simultaneously. That's the whole mental shift: stop thinking "loop over all pixels," start thinking "what's true for one pixel, anywhere."

    The catch: each painter works alone and can't peek at what its neighbor painted (they all go at once). That one rule explains most of what's weird about shader programming.

    The Core Problem

    Your iPhone screen has ~2.7 million pixels (1179 x 2556 on iPhone 15 Pro). At 120fps, that's 324 million pixel calculations per second. A CPU processes things one at a time (or a few at a time with multiple cores). A GPU processes thousands at a time.

    CPU: The Smart Worker

    CPU = 6 very fast, very smart workers
     Each can handle any task
     They work on tasks one by one (mostly)

Task: Color 1 million pixels
CPU approach: Worker 1 colors pixel 1, then pixel 2, then pixel 3...
Time: ~16ms (barely makes 60fps)

    The CPU is great at complex, sequential logic — if/else chains, branching, state machines. But it's terrible at doing the same simple operation millions of times.

    GPU: The Army of Simple Workers

    GPU = 10,000+ simple workers (Apple GPU has thousands of ALUs)
     Each can only do basic math
     But they ALL work simultaneously

Task: Color 1 million pixels
GPU approach: All 10,000 workers color their assigned pixels at the same time
Time: ~0.1ms

    The GPU is terrible at complex branching but incredible at doing the same operation on different data simultaneously. This is called SIMD (Single Instruction, Multiple Data).

    What is a Shader?

    A shader is a small program that runs on the GPU. It's called a "shader" because originally these programs were used to calculate lighting and shading in 3D graphics. Today they do much more.

    You write ONE function.
The GPU runs it on EVERY pixel (or vertex, or data point) simultaneously.

    Think of it like this:

    // CPU approach (Swift)
for y in 0..<screenHeight {
    for x in 0..<screenWidth {
        pixels[x][y] = calculateColor(x, y)  // Sequential: one at a time
    }
}

// GPU approach (Mental model)
// The GPU calls YOUR function for every pixel at the same time
// You write the function for ONE pixel:
func calculateColor(x: Int, y: Int) -> Color {
    return .red  // This runs on ALL pixels simultaneously
}

    Three Types of Shaders

    1. Fragment Shader (Pixel Shader)

    Runs once per pixel. Determines the final color of each pixel on screen.

    Input: pixel position, textures, uniforms
Output: color (RGBA)
Use: Visual effects, post-processing, materials

    This is what you'll use most for iOS visual effects.

    2. Vertex Shader

    Runs once per vertex (corner point of a triangle). Transforms 3D geometry.

    Input: vertex position, normals, texture coordinates
Output: transformed position
Use: 3D transformations, geometry animation, mesh deformation

    3. Compute Shader (Kernel)

    General-purpose GPU computation. Not tied to rendering.

    Input: any data (textures, buffers)
Output: any data
Use: Image processing, physics simulation, machine learning

    Metal Shading Language (MSL)

    Apple's shader language is called Metal Shading Language (MSL). It's based on C++14 with GPU-specific additions.

    C++14 syntax
+ GPU-specific types (float4, half4, texture2d)
+ GPU-specific qualifiers ([[position]], [[texture(0)]])
+ GPU-specific functions (sample, write, threadgroup barriers)
- No dynamic memory allocation
- No recursion
- No virtual functions
- No exceptions

    MSL files have the .metal extension and are compiled by Xcode as part of your build.

    The Rendering Pipeline (Simplified)

    1. Your Swift code sets up data (vertices, textures, parameters)
         |
2. Data is sent to the GPU
         |
3. VERTEX SHADER runs on each vertex
   - Transforms 3D positions to 2D screen positions
         |
4. Rasterizer (hardware, not programmable)
   - Determines which pixels each triangle covers
   - Interpolates vertex data across the triangle
         |
5. FRAGMENT SHADER runs on each pixel
   - Determines the final color
         |
6. Pixels written to framebuffer -> displayed on screen

    For 2D effects (like blur, color grading, distortion), you typically only need a fragment shader operating on a full-screen quad.

    Why This Matters for iOS

    Every visual effect on your iPhone runs through the GPU: - UIVisualEffectView blur? GPU fragment shader. - SwiftUI .shadow()? GPU fragment shader. - Core Animation .cornerRadius? GPU rendering. - Photos app filters? GPU compute shaders. - Every single frame of every animation? GPU.

    When you write Metal shaders, you're writing code at the same level Apple uses for all of iOS.

    Key Concepts to Remember

    Concept Meaning
    Parallel execution Your shader function runs on thousands of pixels simultaneously
    No shared state Each pixel's computation is independent — you can't read what another pixel calculated
    Per-pixel thinking You write logic for ONE pixel; the GPU handles the parallelism
    SIMD Same instruction, different data — the GPU runs your code on different positions/pixels at once
    Framebuffer The final image buffer that gets displayed on screen

    Exercises

    1. Mental model: If you had to turn every pixel on screen to blue, how would you describe it as a shader? (Answer: a function that takes a position and returns blue, regardless of position)

    2. Think parallel: Why can't a blur shader read the result of blurring a neighboring pixel? (Answer: all pixels are computed simultaneously — neighbor hasn't been computed yet when you run)

    3. Count: Your iPhone runs at 120fps with 2.7M pixels. How many fragment shader invocations happen per second? (Answer: 324 million)

    Checkpoint

    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] GPUs process thousands of things simultaneously, CPUs process a few things very fast - [x] A shader is a function that runs on every pixel/vertex/data point in parallel - [x] There are 3 types: fragment (pixel color), vertex (geometry), compute (general purpose) - [x] MSL is based on C++ with GPU-specific additions - [x] You write code for ONE pixel; the GPU handles running it on all pixels

    The foundation

    • CPU = a few fast, clever workers, great at complex step-by-step logic, slow at bulk repetition.
    • GPU = thousands of simple workers running the same instruction on different data at once.
    • SIMD = "Single Instruction, Multiple Data" — the one-rule-many-pixels pattern that makes GPUs fast.
    • Shader = the per-pixel (or per-vertex) rule you write; the GPU runs millions of copies in parallel.
    • No shared state = each pixel computes alone and can't read a neighbor's result — they all run simultaneously.
    • Fragment / vertex / compute = the three shader jobs: pixel color, geometry transform, general number-crunching.
    • MSL = Apple's shader language — C++14 with GPU types and qualifiers, no recursion or dynamic memory.
    Prev← Metal Shading Language (MSL) — Learning RoadmapNext02 — MSL Syntax Basics →

    02 — MSL Syntax Basics

    Start here — the plain idea

    This lesson is just vocabulary. You're not building anything yet — you're learning the words and grammar so the later code reads like English instead of noise.

    The one big idea: shaders live and breathe vectors. A color is four numbers (red, green, blue, alpha) packed together; a position is two or three numbers packed together. So MSL has built-in types like float4 that hold several numbers at once, and math just works on all of them at the same time — add two colors and every channel adds. Think of a vector as a tiny fixed-size list the GPU knows how to crunch in one shot.

    The other thing to absorb: the weird double-bracket tags like [[position]] are just labels — exactly like throws labels "this might fail." They tell Metal "this value is the pixel position" or "this is texture slot 0," so the GPU knows how to wire data in. Don't memorize the list; just recognize that brackets = plumbing labels.

    MSL is C++14 with GPU-specific extensions. If you know Swift, you'll find it familiar but with some key differences.

    Swift vs MSL Cheat Sheet

    Swift MSL Notes
    Float float 32-bit float
    Double — GPUs don't use double (too slow)
    Int int 32-bit integer
    UInt uint 32-bit unsigned integer
    Bool bool Boolean
    — half 16-bit float (GPU-optimized, use this!)
    SIMD2<Float> float2 2-component vector
    SIMD3<Float> float3 3-component vector
    SIMD4<Float> float4 4-component vector (RGBA color!)
    func return-type + name C-style function declaration
    let const Immutable
    var plain type Mutable

    Scalar Types

    // Floating point (use these most)
float  x = 1.0;    // 32-bit — standard precision
half   y = 1.0h;   // 16-bit — faster, use when precision isn't critical
// Note the 'h' suffix for half literals

// Integer
int    a = 42;     // 32-bit signed
uint   b = 42u;    // 32-bit unsigned
short  c = 42;     // 16-bit signed
ushort d = 42u;    // 16-bit unsigned

// Boolean
bool flag = true;

    Performance tip: half (16-bit float) is ~2x faster than float (32-bit) on Apple GPUs. Use half for colors and visual effects, float for positions and coordinates.

    Vector Types — The Most Important Thing in MSL

    Vectors are how you work with colors, positions, and directions. They're the bread and butter of shader programming.

    Declaring Vectors

    // 2-component (positions, UVs)
float2 position = float2(100.0, 200.0);
float2 uv = float2(0.5, 0.5);

// 3-component (RGB colors, 3D positions)
float3 color = float3(1.0, 0.0, 0.0);  // Red
float3 position3d = float3(x, y, z);

// 4-component (RGBA colors, homogeneous coordinates)
float4 red = float4(1.0, 0.0, 0.0, 1.0);    // Red, fully opaque
half4  blue = half4(0.0, 0.0, 1.0, 1.0);     // Blue, half precision

// All components same value
float4 white = float4(1.0);  // = float4(1.0, 1.0, 1.0, 1.0)
float4 clear = float4(0.0);  // = float4(0.0, 0.0, 0.0, 0.0)

    Accessing Components — Swizzling

    This is one of the most powerful features. You can access vector components by name and rearrange them freely:

    float4 color = float4(1.0, 0.5, 0.2, 1.0);

// Access individual components
float r = color.r;    // 1.0 (r = first component)
float g = color.g;    // 0.5
float b = color.b;    // 0.2
float a = color.a;    // 1.0

// Same components, different names (both work!)
float x = color.x;    // 1.0 (x = first component)
float y = color.y;    // 0.5
float z = color.z;    // 0.2
float w = color.w;    // 1.0

// SWIZZLING — rearrange components!
float3 rgb = color.rgb;       // float3(1.0, 0.5, 0.2) — drop alpha
float2 rg = color.rg;         // float2(1.0, 0.5)
float3 bgr = color.bgr;       // float3(0.2, 0.5, 1.0) — reversed!
float4 rrrr = color.rrrr;     // float4(1.0, 1.0, 1.0, 1.0) — repeat!
float2 yx = color.yx;         // float2(0.5, 1.0) — swapped!

    Swizzling rules: - You can use .xyzw or .rgba naming (don't mix them) - You can repeat components: .rrr, .xxxx - You can reorder: .bgr, .zyx - The result type matches the number of components: .rg = float2, .rgb = float3

    Vector Math

    All math operations work component-wise:

    float4 a = float4(1.0, 2.0, 3.0, 4.0);
float4 b = float4(2.0, 3.0, 4.0, 5.0);

float4 sum = a + b;        // float4(3.0, 5.0, 7.0, 9.0)
float4 product = a * b;    // float4(2.0, 6.0, 12.0, 20.0)
float4 scaled = a * 2.0;   // float4(2.0, 4.0, 6.0, 8.0)
float4 mixed = a * 0.5 + b * 0.5;  // Average of a and b

    Matrix Types

    float2x2 mat2;   // 2x2 matrix
float3x3 mat3;   // 3x3 matrix
float4x4 mat4;   // 4x4 matrix (standard transform matrix)

// Create identity-like
float4x4 transform = float4x4(1.0);

// Matrix * vector
float4 transformed = transform * position;

    Built-in Functions (The Essentials)

    Math Functions

    // Clamp to range [0, 1]
float clamped = clamp(value, 0.0, 1.0);
// Same as: clamped = min(max(value, 0.0), 1.0)

// Clamp to [0, 1] shorthand
float sat = saturate(value);  // Metal-specific, same as clamp(value, 0.0, 1.0)

// Linear interpolation (LERP)
float mixed = mix(a, b, t);  // = a * (1-t) + b * t
// t=0 returns a, t=1 returns b, t=0.5 returns average
// Works on vectors too:
float4 blended = mix(color1, color2, 0.3);  // 30% of color2

// Smooth interpolation (S-curve)
float smooth = smoothstep(edge0, edge1, x);
// Returns 0 when x < edge0
// Returns 1 when x > edge1
// Smooth curve between edge0 and edge1
// Used EVERYWHERE for soft transitions

// Step function (hard edge)
float hard = step(edge, x);
// Returns 0 when x < edge
// Returns 1 when x >= edge

    Trigonometry

    float s = sin(angle);        // Sine
float c = cos(angle);        // Cosine
float t = tan(angle);        // Tangent
float a = atan2(y, x);       // Angle from coordinates
float2 sc = float2(cos(a), sin(a));  // Point on unit circle

    Distance & Length

    float len = length(vec);              // Magnitude of vector
float dist = distance(point1, point2); // Distance between points
float3 dir = normalize(vec);          // Unit vector (length = 1)
float d = dot(a, b);                  // Dot product
float3 c = cross(a, b);              // Cross product (3D only)

    Useful Shortcuts

    float mn = min(a, b);         // Minimum
float mx = max(a, b);         // Maximum
float ab = abs(value);        // Absolute value
float fr = fract(value);      // Fractional part (value - floor(value))
float fl = floor(value);      // Round down
float cl = ceil(value);       // Round up
float sg = sign(value);       // -1, 0, or 1
float pw = pow(base, exp);    // Power
float sq = sqrt(value);       // Square root
float rs = rsqrt(value);      // 1/sqrt (faster than sqrt on GPU)

    Function Qualifiers — What Makes a Shader a Shader

    These special qualifiers tell Metal which functions are shader entry points:

    // FRAGMENT SHADER — runs per pixel
fragment float4 myFragmentShader(/* params */) {
    return float4(1, 0, 0, 1); // Return a color
}

// VERTEX SHADER — runs per vertex
vertex float4 myVertexShader(/* params */) {
    return float4(0, 0, 0, 1); // Return a position
}

// COMPUTE SHADER (kernel) — runs per thread
kernel void myComputeShader(/* params */) {
    // Write to textures/buffers
}

    Regular helper functions use no qualifier:

    // Regular function — called by shaders, not an entry point
float3 adjustBrightness(float3 color, float amount) {
    return color * amount;
}

    Attribute Qualifiers — How Data Gets In

    fragment float4 myShader(
    float4 position [[position]],     // Pixel position from rasterizer
    texture2d<float> tex [[texture(0)]], // First texture
    texture2d<float> tex2 [[texture(1)]], // Second texture
    sampler s [[sampler(0)]],          // Texture sampler
    constant float &time [[buffer(0)]], // Uniform data from Swift
    constant MyParams &params [[buffer(1)]] // Struct from Swift
) {
    // ...
}

kernel void myKernel(
    uint2 gid [[thread_position_in_grid]],  // Which thread am I?
    uint2 tid [[thread_position_in_threadgroup]], // Position within group
    uint2 tgid [[threadgroup_position_in_grid]]   // Which group am I in?
) {
    // ...
}

    Key Attributes

    Attribute Meaning
    [[position]] Fragment's pixel position (from vertex shader)
    [[texture(n)]] nth texture binding
    [[sampler(n)]] nth sampler binding
    [[buffer(n)]] nth buffer binding
    [[thread_position_in_grid]] Compute shader: global thread ID
    [[stage_in]] Interpolated vertex output
    [[color(n)]] Render target color attachment

    Control Flow

    // If/else (same as C++)
if (uv.x > 0.5) {
    color = float4(1, 0, 0, 1);
} else {
    color = float4(0, 0, 1, 1);
}

// Ternary (prefer this — avoids GPU branching penalty)
color = (uv.x > 0.5) ? red : blue;

// For loops
for (int i = 0; i < 10; i++) {
    sum += values[i];
}

// WARNING: Avoid complex branching!
// GPUs execute BOTH branches and discard one.
// Use math (step, smoothstep, mix) instead of if/else when possible.

    Performance rule: Replace if (condition) { a } else { b } with mix(b, a, step(threshold, value)) when possible. The GPU processes both branches regardless — mix avoids the penalty.

    A Complete Minimal Shader

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

// This shader turns every pixel red
fragment float4 allRed(float4 position [[position]]) {
    return float4(1.0, 0.0, 0.0, 1.0); // RGBA: red, no green, no blue, fully opaque
}

    That's it. That's a complete, valid Metal shader. Everything else builds on top of this.

    Exercises

    1. Vector math: What is float4(1, 0.5, 0, 1).rgb * 2.0? (Answer: float3(2.0, 1.0, 0.0))

    2. Swizzling: If color = float4(0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8), what is color.abgr? (Answer: float4(0.8, 0.6, 0.4, 0.2))

    3. mix: What does mix(float3(1,0,0), float3(0,0,1), 0.5) return? (Answer: float3(0.5, 0.0, 0.5) — purple, the average of red and blue)

    4. smoothstep: What does smoothstep(0.3, 0.7, 0.5) return? (Answer: 0.5 — it's in the exact middle of the transition range)

    Checkpoint

    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] MSL scalar types: float, half, int, uint, bool - [x] Vector types: float2, float3, float4 and component access (.rgba, .xyzw) - [x] Swizzling: rearranging vector components (.bgr, .rrr) - [x] Key functions: mix, smoothstep, step, clamp, saturate, length, distance, normalize - [x] Shader qualifiers: fragment, vertex, kernel - [x] Attribute qualifiers: [[position]], [[texture(0)]], [[buffer(0)]] - [x] Prefer half over float for colors (2x faster on Apple GPUs) - [x] Avoid if/else branching; use math functions instead

    The foundation

    • Vector type (float2/3/4) = a small bundle of numbers; one holds a color, a position, or a direction.
    • Swizzling = grabbing or rearranging a vector's parts by name, like color.rgb or color.bgr.
    • Component-wise math = +, *, etc. apply to every slot at once — add two colors, every channel adds.
    • half = a 16-bit float; about twice as fast as float on Apple GPUs, perfect for colors.
    • Qualifier (fragment/vertex/kernel) = the word that marks a function as a shader entry point and says which kind.
    • Attribute ([[position]], [[buffer(0)]]) = a plumbing label that tells Metal what a parameter is and where its data comes from.
    • mix / smoothstep / step = the blend toolkit: straight fade, smooth S-curve fade, and a hard on/off switch.
    • Branchless thinking = prefer math over if/else, because the GPU runs both branches and throws one away.

    02a — UV & the Shader Toolkit (the words you'll see everywhere)

    Start here — the plain idea

    Before you write your first shader, it helps to know the handful of words that show up in every shader you'll ever read: uv, float2/half4, .rgb, and a small pile of built-in functions like mix, step, distance, smoothstep. Learn these once and shader code stops looking like hieroglyphics — you'll read float2 uv = position / size and just know what it means.

    Here's the good news: you already learned most of the math in the Math for Interactions course. mix is just lerp. smoothstep, clamp, distance, sin/cos — same ideas, same behavior. This lesson is mostly teaching you the MSL spelling of tools you already own, plus the one genuinely new idea: uv.

    Think of a shader as a function the GPU runs once per pixel, in parallel, millions of times. Each run knows where it is and has to answer what color am I? Everything below is the vocabulary for expressing that answer.

    The one new idea: uv

    A shader runs per pixel, so the first thing a pixel needs is its own address. uv is that address, but rescaled to a tidy 0…1 range instead of raw pixel counts.

    float2 uv = position / size;   // position is in points; size is the view's size
// uv.x: 0.0 at the left  → 1.0 at the right
// uv.y: 0.0 at the top   → 1.0 at the bottom

    Why bother normalizing? Because 0…1 doesn't care about screen size. A rule like "left half red, right half blue" is uv.x < 0.5 — and that works identically on a tiny watch or a huge iPad. If you used raw pixels you'd have to know the exact width every time. uv makes your shader resolution-independent. (The name is a leftover from texture mapping — historically the two texture axes were called u and v instead of x and y. That's the whole story; there's no deeper meaning.)

    Two wrinkles you'll meet soon (full detail in 04-coordinates-and-color):

    • Centered coordinates. For anything radial (circles, glows), it's handy to put (0,0) in the middle: float2 c = uv - 0.5; now ranges −0.5…0.5 from the center.
    • Aspect ratio. If the view isn't square, one unit of uv.x covers more pixels than one unit of uv.y, so a "circle" comes out as an oval. You fix it by scaling x by width/height. Don't worry about it yet — just know that's why circles sometimes look squished.

    The vector types (float2, half4, and friends)

    MSL leans on small fixed-size vectors constantly, because positions and colors are vectors.

    float2 p = float2(1.0, 0.5);        // 2 numbers — a point or size (x, y)
float3 n = float3(0.0, 1.0, 0.0);   // 3 numbers — often a direction or an RGB color
float4 c = float4(1.0, 0.0, 0.0, 1.0); // 4 numbers — an RGBA color (r, g, b, a)

float2 middle = float2(0.5);        // "splat": same value in every slot → (0.5, 0.5)

    float vs half. half is a smaller 16-bit number; float is the full 32-bit one. On Apple GPUs half is roughly twice as fast, so the convention is: use half4 for colors (they don't need much precision) and float/float2 for positions and math (which do). That's why SwiftUI color shaders return half4 but take a float2 position. The little h suffix — 1.0h — just means "this literal is a half."

    Swizzling — grab or reorder components with a dot

    This is the one bit of syntax that looks magical at first. You can pluck out or rearrange a vector's parts by naming them:

    float4 color = float4(0.2, 0.4, 0.8, 1.0);

color.r        // 0.2   (same as color.x — rgba and xyzw are interchangeable)
color.rgb      // float3(0.2, 0.4, 0.8) — just the color, drop the alpha
color.a        // 1.0   (the alpha / w)
color.bgr      // float3(0.8, 0.4, 0.2) — reversed! swizzling can reorder
position.xy    // pull two components out of a bigger vector
float3(uv, 0)  // build a float3 from a float2 plus one more number

    .xyzw and .rgba are the same four slots with two names — use .xy when it's a position, .rgb when it's a color. That's it.

    The built-in functions you'll see constantly

    You already know what most of these do from Math for Interactions — here's the MSL name and the one-line reminder. Deep versions live in 05-math-for-shaders.

    MSL Plain meaning You knew it as
    mix(a, b, t) blend from a to b by t (0…1) lerp
    clamp(x, lo, hi) pin x inside a range clamp
    saturate(x) shortcut for clamp(x, 0, 1) clamp to 0…1
    step(edge, x) hard cutoff: 0 below edge, 1 above a hard if
    smoothstep(e0, e1, x) soft 0→1 ramp with eased ends smoothstep
    fract(x) the part after the decimal point → repeats 0…1 "wrap around"
    floor(x) / ceil(x) round down / up to a whole number —
    abs(x) distance from zero (drop the sign) abs
    min(a,b) / max(a,b) the smaller / larger of two —
    length(v) how long a vector is vector magnitude
    distance(a, b) how far apart two points are distance
    normalize(v) same direction, length 1 unit vector
    dot(a, b) how aligned two directions are (−1…1) dot product
    sin(x) / cos(x) smooth −1…1 waves sin / cos
    pow(x, y) x to the power y (curves, falloff) pow

    Two you'll reach for most: mix (blend two colors) and smoothstep (make an edge soft instead of jagged). If you only memorize two, memorize those.

    Read a real shader with your new vocabulary

    Here's the circle shader from the next lesson. With the toolkit above, every line is now readable:

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 circle(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size) {
    float2 uv = position / size;                    // pixel address → 0…1
    float d = distance(uv, float2(0.5));            // how far this pixel is from center
    float mask = 1.0 - smoothstep(0.28, 0.32, d);   // 1 inside radius, 0 outside, soft edge
    half3 c = mix(half3(0.05h, 0.05h, 0.1h),        // dark background…
                  half3(0.0h, 0.8h, 1.0h),          // …cyan fill
                  half(mask));                       // blend by the mask
    return half4(c, 1.0h);                          // pack rgb + full alpha
}

    Line by line: get this pixel's uv, measure its distance from the center, turn that distance into a soft 0-or-1 mask with smoothstep, mix the background and fill colors by that mask, and return the result as a half4. That's a complete shape-drawing shader, and it's only the words from this lesson. Once these read as plain English, the whole course opens up.

    The foundation

    • uv = a pixel's own address rescaled to 0…1 (position / size), so your shader ignores screen resolution.
    • float2/3/4 = fixed-size number bundles for points, directions, and colors; float2(0.5) splats one value into all slots.
    • half vs float = half is the faster 16-bit type for colors (half4); float is the precise 32-bit type for positions and math.
    • swizzling = grab or reorder parts with a dot — .rgb, .a, .xy, .bgr; .xyzw and .rgba are the same slots.
    • mix = MSL's name for lerp — blend two values/colors by a 0…1 amount (your #1 tool).
    • smoothstep = the soft-edge ramp; use it instead of step whenever you want no jaggies.
    • distance / length / normalize / dot = the vector toolkit for "how far," "how long," "which direction," "how aligned."
    • you already know the math — this lesson is mostly the MSL spelling of tools from the seven building blocks.

    Related

    • Next, put it to work: 03-your-first-shader
    • Coordinates & color in depth: 04-coordinates-and-color
    • Every function, fully explained: 05-math-for-shaders

    03 — Your First Shader: From File to Screen

    Start here — the plain idea

    This is the "hello world" moment: you'll get an actual red screen, then bend it into gradients and shapes. The point isn't the red — it's seeing the whole chain from a .metal file to glowing pixels.

    Here's the modern mental model. In SwiftUI you almost never build the GPU pipeline by hand. You write one tiny function in a .metal file, tag it with the [[ stitchable ]] keyword, and that single word makes the function appear on the Swift side inside ShaderLibrary — no device, no command queue, no render loop. Then you paint it onto any view with one modifier: .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.yourFunction()). Think of [[ stitchable ]] as a label that means "this shader is allowed to plug into SwiftUI," exactly like throws labels "this might fail."

    A colorEffect shader runs once per pixel and answers the only question that matters: what color is this pixel? The magic ingredient that makes shapes possible is uv — each pixel's address turned into a 0..1 fraction. Once a pixel knows "I'm at (0.5, 0.5)," it can decide "am I inside the circle?" That's how a per-pixel color rule becomes gradients, splits, and circles.

    Time to write a shader, hook it up with one line, and see pixels on screen.

    Step 1: Create the Metal File

    In Xcode: File > New > File > Metal File. Name it Shaders.metal.

    #include <metal_stdlib>
#include <SwiftUI/SwiftUI.h>     // gives you SwiftUI::Layer + the stitchable entry points
using namespace metal;

// [[ stitchable ]] is the whole trick: it tells Xcode to expose this function
// to SwiftUI. At build time Xcode scans every .metal file, finds each
// [[ stitchable ]] function, and generates a matching entry in `ShaderLibrary`.
//
// A colorEffect shader's signature is fixed:
//   half4 name(float2 position, half4 color)
//   - position = this pixel's location in POINTS (0,0 at top-left)
//   - color    = the pixel's current color (from the view you attach to)
//   - return   = the new color for this pixel
//
[[ stitchable ]] half4 solidColor(float2 position, half4 color) {
    return half4(1.0, 0.0, 0.0, 1.0);   // red, ignoring the incoming color
}

    That's the entire GPU side. No vertex shader, no full-screen triangle — SwiftUI already drew the view; your function just recolors its pixels.

    Step 2: Hook It Up (one line)

    import SwiftUI

struct ContentView: View {
    var body: some View {
        Rectangle()                                   // any view works: Color, Image, Text…
            .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.solidColor())  // run your shader over its pixels
            .ignoresSafeArea()
    }
}

    ShaderLibrary.solidColor is the function you marked [[ stitchable ]] — it showed up here automatically because of that keyword. .colorEffect runs it once per pixel of the Rectangle.

    Step 3: Run It

    Build and run. You should see a solid red screen. That's your fragment function running on every pixel of the rectangle, returning red. No renderer, no draw loop — SwiftUI owns all of that now.

    Step 4: Make It Interesting

    The incoming position is in points, so to get resolution-independent uv (0..1) you divide by the view's size. Pass the size in as an extra argument — any parameters after the required position, color become your custom inputs:

    Gradient

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 gradient(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size) {
    float2 uv = position / size;                 // 0 (left/top) .. 1 (right/bottom)
    return half4(half(uv.x), 0.0h, 0.0h, 1.0h);  // black on left → red on right
}

    GeometryReader { geo in
    Rectangle()
        .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.gradient(.float2(geo.size)))  // hand the size to the shader
}

    Two-Color Gradient

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 twoColorGradient(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size) {
    float2 uv = position / size;
    half3 top = half3(0.1h, 0.1h, 0.4h);   // dark blue
    half3 bot = half3(1.0h, 0.5h, 0.2h);   // orange
    return half4(mix(top, bot, half(uv.y)), 1.0h);
}

    Horizontal Split

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 horizontalSplit(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size) {
    float2 uv = position / size;
    half side = half(step(0.5, uv.x));     // 0 on the left half, 1 on the right
    half3 c = mix(half3(1.0h,0.0h,0.0h), half3(0.0h,0.0h,1.0h), side);
    return half4(c, 1.0h);
}

    Circle

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 circle(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size) {
    float2 uv = position / size;
    float d = distance(uv, float2(0.5));           // distance from center
    float mask = 1.0 - smoothstep(0.28, 0.32, d);  // soft edge at radius ~0.3
    half3 c = mix(half3(0.05h,0.05h,0.1h),         // dark background
                  half3(0.0h,0.8h,1.0h),           // cyan
                  half(mask));
    return half4(c, 1.0h);
}

    Same Swift call each time — swap the function name and pass .float2(geo.size):

    GeometryReader { geo in
    Rectangle().colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.circle(.float2(geo.size)))
}

    Tidier size without GeometryReader: .visualEffect { content, proxy in content.colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.circle(.float2(proxy.size))) } gives you the size inline. See 18-metal-in-swiftui.

    Passing more data

    Every argument after position, color is yours. Feed them from Swift with typed helpers — .float(_), .float2(_), .color(_), .image(_). For animation, wrap the view in TimelineView(.animation) and pass elapsed seconds:

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 pulse(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size, float time) {
    float2 uv = position / size;
    float d = distance(uv, float2(0.5));
    float ring = 0.5 + 0.5 * sin(d * 40.0 - time * 4.0);  // moving rings
    return half4(half3(ring), 1.0h);
}

    TimelineView(.animation) { tl in
    let t = tl.date.timeIntervalSinceReferenceDate
    GeometryReader { geo in
        Rectangle().colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.pulse(.float2(geo.size), .float(t)))
    }
}

    The three effect flavors (colorEffect, distortionEffect, layerEffect) and every argument type are covered in depth in 18-metal-in-swiftui.

    What's happening underneath (the full Metal pipeline)

    You didn't have to build any of this — but it's worth seeing once so the stitchable shortcut makes sense. To draw pixels from scratch (with no SwiftUI view to attach to), the GPU can only draw triangles, so you cover the screen with one oversized triangle and run a vertex shader to place its corners, then a fragment shader per pixel:

    struct VertexOut {
    float4 position [[position]];  // clip-space position
    float2 uv;                     // 0..1 texture coordinate
};

vertex VertexOut fullscreenVertex(uint vertexID [[vertex_id]]) {
    VertexOut out;
    out.uv = float2((vertexID << 1) & 2, vertexID & 2);
    out.position = float4(out.uv * float2(2, -2) + float2(-1, 1), 0, 1);
    return out;
}

fragment float4 solidColorFrag(VertexOut in [[stage_in]]) {
    return float4(1.0, 0.0, 0.0, 1.0);
}

    Driving that requires the manual pipeline — a MTLDevice, a command queue, a compiled MTLRenderPipelineState, and a per-frame draw loop (command buffer → encoder → draw → present → commit). You only need this for full Metal apps: custom render passes, games, compute, or hosting Metal inside UIKit. The whole setup lives in 17-metal-in-uikit; compute-style pipelines are in 08-compute-shaders.

    The full-screen triangle trick (for the pipeline version)

    vertexID=0: uv=(0,0) -> position=(-1, 1)   Top-left
vertexID=1: uv=(2,0) -> position=( 3, 1)   Far right (off-screen)
vertexID=2: uv=(0,2) -> position=(-1,-3)   Far bottom (off-screen)

     0-----------1 (off screen right)
     |  /screen/ |
     | /      /  |
     |/______/   |
     2           |
     (off screen bottom)

    The GPU clips the oversized triangle to the viewport, so 3 vertices fill the screen — cheaper than two triangles because there's no shared diagonal edge. For SwiftUI effects you never write this; SwiftUI supplies the geometry and calls your [[ stitchable ]] function directly.

    Exercises

    1. Color play: Change solidColor to return purple (half4(0.5, 0.0, 0.8, 1.0)).

    2. Diagonal gradient: Make a gradient from top-left (black) to bottom-right (white). Hint: (uv.x + uv.y) / 2.0.

    3. Concentric rings: Modify circle to show alternating black/white rings. Hint: fract(d * 10.0) then step(0.5, …).

    4. Checkerboard: step(0.5, fract(uv.x * 8.0)) gives stripes; XOR the horizontal and vertical patterns.

    5. Animate it: Feed pulse a time uniform via TimelineView(.animation) and watch the rings move.

    Checkpoint

    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] [[ stitchable ]] is what makes an MSL function appear in Swift's ShaderLibrary - [x] .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.name(args…)) runs your shader over a view's pixels — no renderer needed - [x] A colorEffect signature is half4 name(float2 position, half4 color, …custom args) - [x] position is in points; divide by size (passed as .float2) to get 0..1 uv - [x] Extra arguments come from .float(), .float2(), .color(), .image() - [x] TimelineView(.animation) supplies elapsed time for animated shaders - [x] The manual MTLDevice/pipeline/draw-loop is only for full Metal apps (UIKit, games, compute)

    The foundation

    • [[ stitchable ]] = a label that makes an MSL function visible to SwiftUI's ShaderLibrary — the one keyword that "wires up" a shader.
    • ShaderLibrary = the auto-generated bridge; every [[ stitchable ]] function shows up here by name at build time.
    • .colorEffect = run a per-pixel shader over any view's pixels with one modifier — the modern "file to screen" path.
    • colorEffect signature = half4 name(float2 position, half4 color, …); params after color are your custom inputs.
    • uv = position / size, each pixel's address as a 0..1 fraction, so the code doesn't care about resolution.
    • argument helpers = .float, .float2, .color, .image pack Swift values for the shader; TimelineView(.animation) feeds time.
    • full Metal pipeline = MTLDevice + command queue + MTLRenderPipelineState + draw loop — needed only for UIKit/games/compute, not SwiftUI effects (see 17-metal-in-uikit).
    • full-screen triangle = the pipeline-only trick to cover the screen with 3 vertices; SwiftUI supplies this for you.
    Prev← 02a — UV & the Shader Toolkit (the words you'll see everywhere)Next04 — Coordinates and Color →

    04 — Coordinates and Color

    Start here — the plain idea

    Every pixel needs two things to do its job: a sense of where it is and a way to say what color it is. This lesson nails down both — the map and the paint.

    For "where," there are a few rulers you can lay over the screen. uv is the friendly one: 0..1 across and down, the same on any device, so your code never cares whether it's an iPhone SE or an iPad. Sometimes you want (0,0) in the center instead, and you stretch the x-axis by the aspect ratio so circles stay round instead of ovals. For "color," it's always four numbers — red, green, blue, alpha — that you can add, multiply, fade between, and tint.

    The quietly powerful idea here is the signed distance field (SDF): instead of drawing a shape, you write a formula that tells each pixel "how far am I from the edge?" Negative means inside, zero means on the line, positive means outside. Once a pixel knows its distance, soft edges, outlines, and even combining shapes become one-liners. Think of it as every pixel asking a shape "how close am I?" and coloring itself accordingly.

    Understanding how position and color work in shader space is essential for everything that follows.

    Coordinate Systems

    UV Space (Normalized)

    (0,0)───────────────(1,0)
  │                    │
  │    UV SPACE        │
  │                    │
  │       (0.5,0.5)    │
  │         center     │
  │                    │
(0,1)───────────────(1,1)
    • Range: 0.0 to 1.0
    • Origin: top-left
    • This is what your fragment shader receives in VertexOut.uv
    • Resolution-independent — same coordinates whether on iPhone SE or iPad Pro

    Pixel Space

    (0,0)────────────────(1179,0)
  │                       │
  │    PIXEL SPACE        │
  │    (actual pixels)    │
  │                       │
(0,2556)──────────(1179,2556)
    • [[position]] gives you pixel coordinates
    • Resolution-dependent
    • Convert between them:
    // Pixel to UV
float2 uv = position.xy / viewportSize;

// UV to pixel
float2 pixel = uv * viewportSize;

    Centered Coordinate System

    Often you want (0,0) at the center with proper aspect ratio:

    fragment float4 centeredCoords(VertexOut in [[stage_in]],
                                constant float2 &resolution [[buffer(0)]]) {
    // Convert UV to centered coordinates
    float2 uv = in.uv;
    uv = uv * 2.0 - 1.0;          // Remap from [0,1] to [-1,1]
    uv.x *= resolution.x / resolution.y;  // Fix aspect ratio

    // Now (0,0) is center, and circles look like circles (not ovals)

    float dist = length(uv);  // Distance from center
    float circle = 1.0 - smoothstep(0.4, 0.42, dist);

    return float4(float3(circle), 1.0);
}

    Why fix aspect ratio? Without it, distance calculations treat horizontal and vertical pixels as equal units. Since screens are taller than wide, a "circle" becomes an oval. Multiplying x by width/height corrects this.

    Color in Shaders

    RGBA

    Colors in Metal are float4 (or half4) with components R, G, B, A:

    float4 red     = float4(1, 0, 0, 1);     // Full red, opaque
float4 green   = float4(0, 1, 0, 1);     // Full green, opaque
float4 blue    = float4(0, 0, 1, 1);     // Full blue, opaque
float4 white   = float4(1, 1, 1, 1);     // All channels max
float4 black   = float4(0, 0, 0, 1);     // All channels zero
float4 clear   = float4(0, 0, 0, 0);     // Fully transparent
float4 gray50  = float4(0.5, 0.5, 0.5, 1);  // 50% gray

// Semi-transparent
float4 transRed = float4(1, 0, 0, 0.5);  // Red at 50% opacity

    Color Mixing

    // Linear interpolation between two colors
float4 sunrise = mix(
    float4(0.1, 0.0, 0.2, 1.0),  // Deep purple
    float4(1.0, 0.6, 0.1, 1.0),  // Warm orange
    in.uv.y                        // Blend by vertical position
);

// Multiply (darkens — used for shadows, tinting)
float4 tinted = baseColor * float4(1.0, 0.9, 0.8, 1.0);  // Warm tint

// Screen (brightens — used for highlights)
float4 screened = 1.0 - (1.0 - baseColor) * (1.0 - highlightColor);

// Additive (pure light addition — used for glows)
float4 glowing = baseColor + glowColor;

    Common Color Operations

    // Brightness
float3 brighter = color.rgb * 1.5;     // 50% brighter
float3 darker = color.rgb * 0.5;       // 50% darker

// Grayscale (perceptual luminance weights)
float luminance = dot(color.rgb, float3(0.2126, 0.7152, 0.0722));
float3 gray = float3(luminance);

// Contrast
float3 contrasted = (color.rgb - 0.5) * contrastAmount + 0.5;

// Saturation
float3 desaturated = mix(float3(luminance), color.rgb, saturationAmount);
// saturationAmount: 0 = grayscale, 1 = original, >1 = oversaturated

// Invert
float3 inverted = 1.0 - color.rgb;

// Hue shift (rotate color components)
// Simple approximation:
float3 hueShifted = color.brg;  // Rotate channels (crude but fast)

    Premultiplied Alpha

    Metal (and Core Animation) use premultiplied alpha by default. This means the RGB values are already multiplied by alpha:

    // Straight alpha (what you might expect):
// RGBA = (1.0, 0.0, 0.0, 0.5) means "red at 50% opacity"

// Premultiplied alpha (what Metal uses):
// RGBA = (0.5, 0.0, 0.0, 0.5) means "red at 50% opacity"
// RGB is pre-multiplied by A: (1.0 * 0.5, 0.0 * 0.5, 0.0 * 0.5, 0.5)

// Convert straight to premultiplied:
float4 premultiplied = float4(color.rgb * color.a, color.a);

// Why? Premultiplied blending is a single operation:
// result = src + dst * (1 - src.a)
// vs straight alpha which needs:
// result = src * src.a + dst * (1 - src.a)  -- two multiplies

    Practical Gradient Recipes

    Radial Gradient

    fragment float4 radialGradient(VertexOut in [[stage_in]]) {
    float2 center = float2(0.5, 0.5);
    float dist = distance(in.uv, center);

    // Normalize distance (0 at center, 1 at edge)
    float t = saturate(dist / 0.5);

    float3 innerColor = float3(1.0, 0.8, 0.0);  // Gold
    float3 outerColor = float3(0.1, 0.0, 0.2);  // Deep purple

    return float4(mix(innerColor, outerColor, t), 1.0);
}

    Angular Gradient (Conic)

    fragment float4 angularGradient(VertexOut in [[stage_in]]) {
    float2 centered = in.uv - 0.5;

    // atan2 returns angle in radians (-pi to pi)
    float angle = atan2(centered.y, centered.x);

    // Normalize to 0-1
    float t = (angle / (2.0 * M_PI_F)) + 0.5;

    // Rainbow
    float3 color = float3(
        sin(t * 6.28) * 0.5 + 0.5,
        sin(t * 6.28 + 2.09) * 0.5 + 0.5,
        sin(t * 6.28 + 4.19) * 0.5 + 0.5
    );

    return float4(color, 1.0);
}

    Multi-Stop Gradient

    fragment float4 multiStopGradient(VertexOut in [[stage_in]]) {
    float t = in.uv.y;  // Vertical position

    float3 color;
    if (t < 0.33) {
        color = mix(float3(1,0,0), float3(0,1,0), t / 0.33);         // Red -> Green
    } else if (t < 0.66) {
        color = mix(float3(0,1,0), float3(0,0,1), (t - 0.33) / 0.33); // Green -> Blue
    } else {
        color = mix(float3(0,0,1), float3(1,0,0), (t - 0.66) / 0.33); // Blue -> Red
    }

    return float4(color, 1.0);
}

    Signed Distance Fields (SDF) — Drawing Shapes

    SDFs are a powerful technique where you define shapes by their distance function. The distance is negative inside, zero at the edge, and positive outside.

    Circle SDF

    float sdCircle(float2 point, float2 center, float radius) {
    return distance(point, center) - radius;
    // Negative = inside the circle
    // Zero = on the edge
    // Positive = outside the circle
}

fragment float4 sdfCircle(VertexOut in [[stage_in]]) {
    float2 uv = in.uv * 2.0 - 1.0;  // Center the coordinates

    float d = sdCircle(uv, float2(0), 0.5);

    // Hard edge
    // float shape = step(d, 0.0);

    // Soft edge (anti-aliased)
    float shape = 1.0 - smoothstep(-0.01, 0.01, d);

    return float4(float3(shape), 1.0);
}

    Rounded Rectangle SDF

    float sdRoundedRect(float2 point, float2 halfSize, float radius) {
    float2 d = abs(point) - halfSize + radius;
    return length(max(d, 0.0)) + min(max(d.x, d.y), 0.0) - radius;
}

fragment float4 sdfRoundedRect(VertexOut in [[stage_in]]) {
    float2 uv = in.uv * 2.0 - 1.0;

    float d = sdRoundedRect(uv, float2(0.6, 0.3), 0.1);
    float shape = 1.0 - smoothstep(-0.005, 0.005, d);

    // Bonus: outline
    float outline = 1.0 - smoothstep(0.0, 0.01, abs(d) - 0.02);

    float3 color = mix(float3(0.1), float3(0.0, 0.5, 1.0), shape);
    color = mix(color, float3(1.0), outline);

    return float4(color, 1.0);
}

    Combining Shapes

    // Union (OR) — combine two shapes
float sdUnion(float d1, float d2) { return min(d1, d2); }

// Intersection (AND) — where both shapes overlap
float sdIntersect(float d1, float d2) { return max(d1, d2); }

// Subtraction — cut one shape from another
float sdSubtract(float d1, float d2) { return max(d1, -d2); }

// Smooth union — blend shapes together
float sdSmoothUnion(float d1, float d2, float k) {
    float h = saturate(0.5 + 0.5 * (d2 - d1) / k);
    return mix(d2, d1, h) - k * h * (1.0 - h);
}

    Exercises

    1. Aspect-correct circle: Draw a circle that looks perfectly round on your device (not oval). Use the aspect ratio correction.

    2. SDF ring: Use the circle SDF to draw a ring (circle outline) with a specific thickness.

    3. Color wheel: Create a color wheel using the angular gradient, where the color is fully saturated at the edge and white at the center.

    4. Vignette: Create a vignette effect (dark edges, bright center) using radial distance.

    Checkpoint

    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] UV space (0,0)-(1,1) vs pixel space vs centered coordinates - [x] Aspect ratio correction: uv.x *= resolution.x / resolution.y - [x] Color as float4(R, G, B, A) with values 0.0-1.0 - [x] mix() for blending, dot() for luminance, premultiplied alpha - [x] Radial, angular, and multi-stop gradients - [x] Signed Distance Fields: distance-based shape rendering - [x] SDF operations: union, intersection, subtraction, smooth union

    The foundation

    • uv space = 0..1 across and down, resolution-independent — the same on any screen size.
    • Pixel space = actual pixel counts from [[position]]; resolution-dependent, convert via position / resolution.
    • Aspect-ratio fix = multiply x by width/height so circles render round instead of stretched into ovals.
    • RGBA = a color is four numbers (red, green, blue, alpha), each 0..1.
    • Premultiplied alpha = Metal stores RGB already multiplied by alpha, which makes blending a single cheap step.
    • SDF (signed distance field) = a formula giving each pixel its distance to a shape's edge: negative inside, 0 on the edge, positive outside.
    • SDF combine ops = min = union, max = intersection, max(a, -b) = subtraction, plus a smooth-union that melts shapes together.
    Prev← 03 — Your First Shader: From File to ScreenNext05 — Essential Math for Shaders →

    05 — Essential Math for Shaders

    Start here — the plain idea

    This is the toolbox lesson. Almost every shader you'll ever read is built from a handful of small math functions, used over and over. Learn what each one feels like and the scary-looking code stops being scary.

    The mental shortcuts: mix is a dimmer slider that fades between two things. step is a light switch — off below a line, on above it. smoothstep is the same switch but with a soft dimmer ramp, which is how you get clean, non-jagged edges. fract chops off the whole-number part, which makes things repeat — that's how one tile becomes a grid. And sin/cos are your wave makers — feed them time and things wobble, orbit, and pulse.

    Two more worth their own sentence. atan2 turns a position into an angle (handy for anything that sweeps around a center). And dot quietly measures "how much do these two directions agree?" — which is the heart of lighting. Don't memorize formulas; build the gut feel for what each knob does.

    You don't need a math degree. You need ~10 functions and the intuition for when to use them. This lesson gives you both.

    The Big Five: Functions You'll Use Every Day

    1. mix(a, b, t) — Linear Interpolation

    The most important function in shader programming. Blends between two values.

    mix(a, b, t) = a * (1.0 - t) + b * t

// t = 0.0  ->  returns a
// t = 0.5  ->  returns (a + b) / 2  (average)
// t = 1.0  ->  returns b
// t = 0.25 ->  returns 75% a + 25% b

    // Blend colors
float3 color = mix(red, blue, 0.5);        // Purple

// Fade based on distance
float alpha = mix(1.0, 0.0, distance);     // Fade out with distance

// Animate between states
float3 pos = mix(startPos, endPos, progress);  // Move from A to B

    Works on all types: float, float2, float3, float4. Each component is interpolated independently.

    2. step(edge, x) — Hard Threshold

    step(edge, x) = (x >= edge) ? 1.0 : 0.0

    // Split screen in half
float mask = step(0.5, uv.x);          // 0 on left, 1 on right

// Threshold a gradient
float bw = step(0.3, grayscale);        // Black below 0.3, white above

// Create a border at specific distance
float border = step(0.49, uv.x) * step(uv.x, 0.51);  // Thin vertical line

    3. smoothstep(edge0, edge1, x) — Smooth Threshold

    smoothstep(edge0, edge1, x):
  x < edge0  ->  0.0
  x > edge1  ->  1.0
  between    ->  smooth S-curve (Hermite interpolation)

    This is step but with a smooth transition instead of a sharp cut. The transition zone is between edge0 and edge1.

    // Soft circle edge
float circle = 1.0 - smoothstep(0.28, 0.32, dist);
// dist < 0.28: fully inside (1.0)
// dist > 0.32: fully outside (0.0)
// 0.28 to 0.32: smooth gradient (anti-aliased edge)

// Fog effect
float fog = smoothstep(0.0, 100.0, depth);

// Smooth color band
float band = smoothstep(0.3, 0.35, uv.y) - smoothstep(0.65, 0.7, uv.y);

    Tip: The width between edge0 and edge1 controls the softness. Narrow = sharper, wide = softer.

    4. clamp(x, minVal, maxVal) / saturate(x)

    clamp(x, 0.0, 1.0)  // Forces x into [0, 1] range
saturate(x)           // Same thing, Metal shorthand (faster on Apple GPUs)

    // Prevent color overflow
float3 color = saturate(brightColor);  // Clamps each component to [0, 1]

// Limit a parameter
float radius = clamp(userRadius, 1.0, 50.0);

    5. fract(x) — Fractional Part

    fract(x) = x - floor(x)
// Always returns a value between 0.0 and 1.0

fract(0.7)  = 0.7
fract(1.7)  = 0.7
fract(2.7)  = 0.7
fract(-0.3) = 0.7

    This creates repeating patterns:

    // Repeating stripes
float stripes = step(0.5, fract(uv.x * 10.0));  // 10 black/white stripes

// Repeating tiles
float2 tileUV = fract(uv * 4.0);  // 4x4 grid of repeated UV spaces

// Sawtooth wave (goes 0->1, 0->1, 0->1...)
float saw = fract(time);

// Triangle wave (goes 0->1->0->1->0...)
float tri = abs(fract(time) * 2.0 - 1.0);

    Trigonometry: Circles, Waves, Rotation

    sin and cos

    // Sine wave: oscillates between -1 and 1
float wave = sin(time);           // Oscillates over time
float wave01 = sin(time) * 0.5 + 0.5;  // Remap to [0, 1]

// Cosine: same as sine but shifted by 90 degrees
// sin and cos together trace a circle:
float2 circlePoint = float2(cos(angle), sin(angle)) * radius;

    Creating Waves

    // Horizontal wave
float wave = sin(uv.x * 20.0) * 0.02;  // 20 waves, 0.02 amplitude
float2 distortedUV = float2(uv.x, uv.y + wave);

// Animated wave
float wave = sin(uv.x * 10.0 + time * 3.0) * 0.05;

// Compound wave (more organic)
float wave = sin(uv.x * 10.0 + time) * 0.03
           + sin(uv.x * 23.0 + time * 1.5) * 0.015
           + sin(uv.x * 7.0 - time * 0.8) * 0.02;

    Rotation

    // Rotate a 2D point around origin
float2 rotate(float2 point, float angle) {
    float c = cos(angle);
    float s = sin(angle);
    return float2(
        point.x * c - point.y * s,
        point.x * s + point.y * c
    );
}

// Usage: rotate UV coordinates
float2 centered = uv - 0.5;         // Move origin to center
float2 rotated = rotate(centered, time);  // Rotate over time
float2 finalUV = rotated + 0.5;     // Move origin back

    atan2 — Angle From Position

    float angle = atan2(y, x);
// Returns angle in radians from -pi to pi
// (0,1) -> pi/2 (straight up)
// (1,0) -> 0 (right)
// (0,-1) -> -pi/2 (down)
// (-1,0) -> pi (left)

// Normalize to [0, 1]:
float normalizedAngle = (atan2(y, x) / (2.0 * M_PI_F)) + 0.5;

    Distance Functions

    // Euclidean distance (standard "as the crow flies")
float d = distance(a, b);  // = length(a - b) = sqrt((a.x-b.x)^2 + (a.y-b.y)^2)

// Length of a vector
float len = length(vec);

// Squared length (FASTER — no sqrt, use for comparisons)
float lenSq = dot(vec, vec);  // = vec.x*vec.x + vec.y*vec.y
// Use when you only need to compare distances:
if (dot(vec, vec) < radius * radius) { ... }  // Faster than length(vec) < radius

// Manhattan distance (grid-based, "taxi cab")
float manhattan = abs(a.x - b.x) + abs(a.y - b.y);
// Creates diamond shapes instead of circles

// Chebyshev distance (chessboard)
float chebyshev = max(abs(a.x - b.x), abs(a.y - b.y));
// Creates square shapes

    Mapping and Remapping

    Remap a Value Between Ranges

    // Remap from [inMin, inMax] to [outMin, outMax]
float remap(float value, float inMin, float inMax, float outMin, float outMax) {
    float t = (value - inMin) / (inMax - inMin);  // Normalize to [0, 1]
    return mix(outMin, outMax, t);                  // Map to output range
}

// Examples:
float uiValue = remap(sin(time), -1.0, 1.0, 0.0, 255.0);  // Sine -> 0-255
float screenX = remap(normalizedX, 0.0, 1.0, -1.0, 1.0);   // UV -> clip space

    Applying Easing (Curved Interpolation)

    // Linear (no easing)
float linear = t;

// Ease in (slow start)
float easeIn = t * t;               // Quadratic
float easeInCubic = t * t * t;      // Cubic (stronger)

// Ease out (slow end)
float easeOut = 1.0 - (1.0 - t) * (1.0 - t);           // Quadratic
float easeOutCubic = 1.0 - pow(1.0 - t, 3.0);           // Cubic

// Ease in-out (slow start and end)
float easeInOut = smoothstep(0.0, 1.0, t);  // smoothstep IS ease in-out!

// Bounce (overshoots then settles)
float bounce = 1.0 - pow(1.0 - t, 3.0) * cos(t * 10.0 * M_PI_F);

// Elastic
float elastic = pow(2.0, -10.0 * t) * sin((t - 0.075) * (2.0 * M_PI_F) / 0.3) + 1.0;

    Modular Arithmetic for Patterns

    // Repeat space (tiles)
float2 tiledUV = fract(uv * float2(4, 4));  // 4x4 tiles

// Repeat with offset (brick pattern)
float2 brickUV = uv * float2(4, 8);
brickUV.x += step(1.0, fmod(brickUV.y, 2.0)) * 0.5;  // Offset every other row
brickUV = fract(brickUV);

// Mirror repeat
float mirrorUV = abs(fract(uv.x * 2.0) * 2.0 - 1.0);  // Ping-pong 0->1->0

    Dot Product — Understanding It Intuitively

    dot(a, b) = a.x*b.x + a.y*b.y + a.z*b.z

    What does it actually measure? - Parallel vectors (same direction): dot = positive (maximum when both are unit vectors: 1.0) - Perpendicular vectors: dot = 0 - Opposite vectors: dot = negative (minimum: -1.0)

    // Lighting: how much does a surface face the light?
float brightness = max(dot(surfaceNormal, lightDirection), 0.0);
// 1.0 = directly facing light (brightest)
// 0.0 = perpendicular or facing away (darkest)

// Luminance: weighted sum of RGB (dot product with weight vector)
float luma = dot(color.rgb, float3(0.2126, 0.7152, 0.0722));

// Projection: how far along a direction?
float projection = dot(point, normalize(direction));

    Quick Reference Card

    BLENDING:    mix(a, b, t)                    Blend between a and b
THRESHOLD:   step(edge, x)                   Hard cut at edge
SOFT EDGE:   smoothstep(lo, hi, x)           Smooth transition
CLAMP:       saturate(x)                     Force to [0, 1]
REPEAT:      fract(x)                        Fractional part (tiling)
WAVE:        sin(x * freq + time * speed)    Oscillation
ROTATE:      float2(c*x - s*y, s*x + c*y)   2D rotation
ANGLE:       atan2(y, x)                     Position to angle
DISTANCE:    length(a - b)                   Euclidean distance
REMAP:       mix(lo, hi, (x-a)/(b-a))       Change value range
POWER:       pow(x, n)                       Curves: n>1 darkens, n<1 brightens

    Exercises

    1. Pulsing circle: Create a circle whose radius oscillates using sin(time). The circle should smoothly grow and shrink.

    2. Rotating gradient: Create a gradient that rotates over time. Hint: rotate the UV coordinates before computing the gradient.

    3. Grid pattern: Create a grid of dots using fract() and distance(). Each tile should contain a centered circle.

    4. Easing comparison: Render 4 horizontal bars, each using a different easing function (linear, ease-in, ease-out, ease-in-out). Animate their width with time.

    Checkpoint

    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] mix for blending, step for hard edges, smoothstep for soft edges - [x] fract creates repeating patterns, saturate clamps to [0,1] - [x] Sine waves for oscillation and animation - [x] 2D rotation using sin/cos - [x] atan2 for getting angles from positions - [x] Distance metrics: Euclidean vs Manhattan vs Chebyshev - [x] Remapping values between ranges - [x] Easing functions for natural-feeling animation - [x] Dot product for lighting and projections

    The foundation

    • mix(a, b, t) = a dimmer slider that fades from a to b; t=0 is all a, t=1 is all b.
    • step(edge, x) = a light switch: 0 below the edge, 1 at or above it.
    • smoothstep(lo, hi, x) = step with a soft ramp; this is how you get clean anti-aliased edges.
    • fract(x) = drop the whole-number part to get 0..1; repeat it and you get tiles, stripes, and grids.
    • sin / cos = wave makers; feed them time to oscillate, and pair them to trace circles or rotate points.
    • atan2(y, x) = turn a position into an angle — the basis for sweeps and conic gradients.
    • dot(a, b) = how much two directions agree (1 same, 0 perpendicular, -1 opposite) — the heart of lighting.
    • Easing = curved interpolation (t*t, pow, smoothstep) that makes motion feel natural instead of robotic.
    Prev← 04 — Coordinates and ColorNext06 — Fragment Shaders Deep Dive →

    06 — Fragment Shaders Deep Dive

    Start here — the plain idea

    A fragment shader is just a tiny function that runs once per pixel and answers one question: what color is this pixel? The GPU runs millions of copies at once. You already met this idea — now we open the hood and look at exactly what goes in and what comes out.

    The whole lesson is really about plumbing: how does each pixel get the stuff it needs to decide its color? Three pipes feed it. [[stage_in]] brings interpolated data from the vertex stage (most importantly the pixel's uv). [[buffer(n)]] brings values from your Swift code — time, touch position, a settings struct — the live dials. And [[texture(n)]] brings images, which you read with a sampler that decides how to fetch pixels (smooth or crisp, what to do at the edges).

    Keep one picture in mind: a pixel is a little worker handed a position, some settings, and maybe a photo, who must shout back one color. Everything else here is just the different ways to hand it those ingredients.

    Fragment shaders are where you'll spend 90% of your time for iOS visual effects. This lesson covers the full function signature, all the ways data gets in, and real patterns.

    The Fragment Function Signature

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

fragment float4 myShader(
    VertexOut in [[stage_in]],                      // Interpolated vertex output
    constant float &time [[buffer(0)]],             // A single float from Swift
    constant float2 &resolution [[buffer(1)]],      // Screen resolution
    texture2d<float> inputTexture [[texture(0)]],   // An image/texture
    sampler texSampler [[sampler(0)]]               // How to read the texture
) {
    // Your per-pixel logic here
    return float4(r, g, b, a);  // MUST return a color
}

    Every parameter is optional except the return type. Let's break each one down.

    [[stage_in]] — Interpolated Vertex Data

    The rasterizer takes the vertex shader's output and interpolates it across the triangle for each pixel. This is how UV coordinates smoothly vary across the screen.

    struct VertexOut {
    float4 position [[position]];  // REQUIRED: pixel position
    float2 uv;                     // UV coordinates (0 to 1)
    float3 normal;                 // Surface normal (for 3D)
    float4 color;                  // Per-vertex color
};

// The rasterizer interpolates ALL fields (except [[position]])
// between the 3 vertices of each triangle.

    [[position]] in the fragment shader gives you the pixel coordinate (not the clip-space position from the vertex shader). Use .xy for the 2D position:

    fragment float4 pixelCoords(VertexOut in [[stage_in]]) {
    float2 pixelPos = in.position.xy;  // e.g., (589.5, 1278.5)
    // .z = depth (0 to 1)
    // .w = 1/w (for perspective correction)
    return float4(1, 0, 0, 1);
}

    [[buffer(n)]] — Data from Swift

    This is how you pass parameters from your Swift code to the shader.

    Swift Side

    // Single value
var time: Float = 0.0
encoder.setFragmentBytes(&time, length: MemoryLayout<Float>.size, index: 0)

// Vector
var resolution = SIMD2<Float>(Float(view.drawableSize.width), Float(view.drawableSize.height))
encoder.setFragmentBytes(&resolution, length: MemoryLayout<SIMD2<Float>>.size, index: 1)

// Custom struct
struct ShaderParams {
    var time: Float
    var resolution: SIMD2<Float>
    var touchPoint: SIMD2<Float>
    var intensity: Float
}
var params = ShaderParams(time: elapsed, resolution: res, touchPoint: touch, intensity: 0.8)
encoder.setFragmentBytes(&params, length: MemoryLayout<ShaderParams>.size, index: 0)

    Metal Side

    // Match the Swift struct exactly
struct ShaderParams {
    float time;
    float2 resolution;
    float2 touchPoint;
    float intensity;
};

fragment float4 interactive(
    VertexOut in [[stage_in]],
    constant ShaderParams &params [[buffer(0)]]
) {
    float2 touch = params.touchPoint / params.resolution;  // Normalize
    float dist = distance(in.uv, touch);
    float glow = params.intensity / (dist * 20.0 + 0.1);

    return float4(float3(glow), 1.0);
}

    Alignment rules: Metal structs must match Swift struct layout. Use Float, SIMD2<Float>, SIMD3<Float>, SIMD4<Float> on the Swift side. Avoid Bool (alignment issues) — use Int32 or Float instead.

    [[texture(n)]] — Image Input

    Swift Side

    // From UIImage
let textureLoader = MTKTextureLoader(device: device)
let texture = try textureLoader.newTexture(cgImage: uiImage.cgImage!, options: [
    .textureUsage: MTLTextureUsage.shaderRead.rawValue,
    .textureStorageMode: MTLStorageMode.shared.rawValue
])
encoder.setFragmentTexture(texture, index: 0)

    Metal Side

    fragment float4 texturedShader(
    VertexOut in [[stage_in]],
    texture2d<float> tex [[texture(0)]],
    sampler s [[sampler(0)]]
) {
    // Sample the texture at UV coordinates
    float4 color = tex.sample(s, in.uv);
    return color;
}

    Creating a Sampler in the Shader (No Swift Config)

    fragment float4 inlinesampler(VertexOut in [[stage_in]],
                               texture2d<float> tex [[texture(0)]]) {
    // Define sampler directly in shader
    constexpr sampler s(
        coord::normalized,           // UVs are 0-1 (not pixel coords)
        address::clamp_to_edge,      // Clamp out-of-bounds reads
        filter::linear               // Bilinear interpolation
    );

    return tex.sample(s, in.uv);
}

    Sampler options:

    Option Values Meaning
    coord normalized, pixel UV range: [0,1] vs [0,width]
    address clamp_to_edge, repeat, mirrored_repeat, clamp_to_zero Out-of-bounds behavior
    filter nearest, linear Pixel interpolation (sharp vs smooth)
    mip_filter none, nearest, linear Mipmap filtering

    Return Type: float4 vs half4

    // Standard precision (32-bit per channel)
fragment float4 standardPrecision(...) {
    return float4(1, 0, 0, 1);
}

// Half precision (16-bit per channel) — FASTER on Apple GPUs
fragment half4 halfPrecision(...) {
    return half4(1, 0, 0, 1);
}

    Use half4 when: - Your render target is 8-bit or 10-bit (most of the time) - You don't need extreme color precision - Performance matters (always)

    Use float4 when: - Rendering to a float32 texture (HDR, compute intermediate) - Doing precision-sensitive calculations

    Real-World Fragment Shader Patterns

    Pattern 1: Post-Processing Pipeline

    Process an existing rendered image:

    fragment half4 postProcess(
    VertexOut in [[stage_in]],
    texture2d<half> sceneTexture [[texture(0)]])
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear);
    half4 color = sceneTexture.sample(s, in.uv);

    // Vignette
    float2 centered = in.uv - 0.5;
    float vignette = 1.0 - dot(centered, centered) * 1.5;
    color.rgb *= vignette;

    // Slight warm tint
    color.r *= 1.05h;
    color.b *= 0.95h;

    return color;
}

    Pattern 2: Procedural Effect (No Input Texture)

    fragment half4 plasma(
    VertexOut in [[stage_in]],
    constant float &time [[buffer(0)]])
{
    float2 uv = in.uv * 10.0;

    float v1 = sin(uv.x + time);
    float v2 = sin(uv.y + time * 0.7);
    float v3 = sin(uv.x + uv.y + time * 0.5);
    float v4 = sin(length(uv - 5.0) + time);

    float v = (v1 + v2 + v3 + v4) * 0.25;

    half3 color = half3(
        sin(v * M_PI_F) * 0.5h + 0.5h,
        sin(v * M_PI_F + 2.094h) * 0.5h + 0.5h,
        sin(v * M_PI_F + 4.189h) * 0.5h + 0.5h
    );

    return half4(color, 1.0h);
}

    Pattern 3: Multi-Texture Compositing

    fragment half4 composite(
    VertexOut in [[stage_in]],
    texture2d<half> background [[texture(0)]],
    texture2d<half> foreground [[texture(1)]],
    texture2d<half> mask [[texture(2)]],
    constant float &blendAmount [[buffer(0)]])
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear);

    half4 bg = background.sample(s, in.uv);
    half4 fg = foreground.sample(s, in.uv);
    half maskValue = mask.sample(s, in.uv).r;

    // Blend using mask and blend amount
    half4 result = mix(bg, fg, maskValue * half(blendAmount));

    return result;
}

    Pattern 4: Touch-Interactive Effect

    struct TouchParams {
    float2 touchPoint;   // Normalized touch position
    float radius;        // Effect radius
    float time;          // Time since touch
    float2 resolution;
};

fragment half4 touchRipple(
    VertexOut in [[stage_in]],
    texture2d<half> tex [[texture(0)]],
    constant TouchParams &touch [[buffer(0)]])
{
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear);

    float2 uv = in.uv;
    float dist = distance(uv, touch.touchPoint);

    // Ripple distortion
    float ripple = sin(dist * 40.0 - touch.time * 8.0);
    float falloff = exp(-dist * 5.0) * exp(-touch.time * 2.0);
    float2 offset = normalize(uv - touch.touchPoint) * ripple * falloff * 0.02;

    half4 color = tex.sample(s, uv + offset);
    return color;
}

    Debugging Fragment Shaders

    Visualize Values as Colors

    // Debug: visualize UV coordinates
return float4(in.uv, 0.0, 1.0);  // R=x, G=y

// Debug: visualize a single value as grayscale
return float4(float3(someValue), 1.0);

// Debug: visualize negative values (blue=negative, red=positive)
return float4(max(someValue, 0.0), 0.0, max(-someValue, 0.0), 1.0);

// Debug: visualize out-of-range values
if (someValue > 1.0) return float4(1, 0, 1, 1);  // Magenta = over 1
if (someValue < 0.0) return float4(0, 1, 1, 1);   // Cyan = under 0

    Common Mistakes

    // WRONG: Forgetting premultiplied alpha
return float4(color.rgb, 0.5);  // Semi-transparent but RGB isn't premultiplied

// RIGHT:
return float4(color.rgb * 0.5, 0.5);  // Premultiplied

// WRONG: Integer division
float half = 1 / 2;  // = 0 (integer division!)

// RIGHT:
float half = 1.0 / 2.0;  // = 0.5

// WRONG: Sampling outside [0,1] without proper address mode
tex.sample(s, uv * 2.0);  // May read garbage if address mode isn't set

// RIGHT: Use clamp_to_edge or repeat
constexpr sampler s(address::clamp_to_edge, filter::linear);

    Exercises

    1. Sepia filter: Write a fragment shader that loads a texture and applies a sepia tone. Formula: multiply the luminance by float3(1.2, 1.0, 0.8).

    2. Split screen: Show the original texture on the left half and a processed version (your choice of effect) on the right half.

    3. Magnifying glass: Where the touch point is, show a zoomed-in view of the texture. Hint: remap UVs near the touch point.

    4. Animated scanlines: Overlay horizontal scanlines that scroll vertically over time.

    Checkpoint

    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] The fragment function signature with all parameter types - [x] [[stage_in]] receives interpolated vertex data - [x] [[buffer(n)]] receives data from Swift (floats, vectors, structs) - [x] [[texture(n)]] receives images; sampled with tex.sample(sampler, uv) - [x] Sampler configuration: coord, address, filter - [x] How to pass custom structs from Swift to Metal - [x] Use half4 return type for better performance - [x] How to debug by visualizing values as colors

    The foundation

    • Fragment shader = a per-pixel function whose only job is to return one color.
    • [[stage_in]] = interpolated data from the vertex stage (uv, normal, etc.), smoothly varied across each triangle.
    • [[buffer(n)]] = values handed in from Swift — time, touch, a settings struct — the live dials of your effect.
    • [[texture(n)]] = an image input, read via tex.sample(sampler, uv).
    • Sampler = the rule for reading a texture: smooth vs crisp (filter) and what to do off the edges (address).
    • Struct alignment = your Metal struct must match the Swift struct's layout exactly, or values arrive scrambled.
    • half4 return = 16-bit color output, faster on Apple GPUs and fine for normal 8/10-bit screens.
    • Debug-as-color = paint a value onto the screen (e.g. float4(uv, 0, 1)) to see what's actually happening.
    Prev← 05 — Essential Math for ShadersNext07 — Vertex Shaders →

    07 — Vertex Shaders

    Start here — the plain idea

    If a fragment shader answers "what color is this pixel?", a vertex shader answers "where on screen does this corner go?" Fragments paint; vertices place. Every 3D model is just a cloud of corner points (vertices) joined into triangles, and the vertex shader runs once per corner to figure out where each one lands.

    The core job is a chain of transforms. A model is born in its own little coordinate world; you slide and spin it into the scene (world space), then look at it through a camera (view space), then squash that into the flat frame of the screen (clip space). Those steps are just matrix multiplications — think of a matrix as a "move/rotate/scale machine" you run a point through. The GPU then fills the triangles between your placed corners and hands each pixel to the fragment shader.

    Two practical notes: for flat 2D effects you skip real geometry entirely and use the full-screen triangle trick. And because the vertex shader can move corners, it's where you make things wobble, explode, or bounce — animation by relocating points.

    Vertex shaders transform geometry — they determine WHERE pixels end up on screen. While fragment shaders decide color, vertex shaders decide position.

    What Vertex Shaders Do

    3D World                    2D Screen
============               ============
    *  * (vertices)   -->     ** (projected)
   / \/ \                   /  \
  *----*                   *----*

Vertex shader transforms each * from 3D world space to 2D screen space

    The Pipeline

    Vertex Data (positions, normals, UVs)
        |
  VERTEX SHADER (runs per vertex)
  - Transform position (model -> world -> camera -> clip space)
  - Pass data to fragment shader
        |
  Rasterizer (hardware)
  - Determines which pixels each triangle covers
  - Interpolates vertex outputs across triangle surface
        |
  FRAGMENT SHADER (runs per pixel)
  - Receives interpolated vertex output
  - Returns final pixel color

    Basic Vertex Shader

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

struct VertexIn {
    float3 position [[attribute(0)]];   // 3D position
    float2 texCoord [[attribute(1)]];   // Texture coordinate
    float3 normal   [[attribute(2)]];   // Surface normal
};

struct VertexOut {
    float4 position [[position]];       // REQUIRED: clip-space position
    float2 texCoord;                    // Passed to fragment shader
    float3 normal;                      // Passed to fragment shader
    float3 worldPosition;              // For lighting calculations
};

struct Uniforms {
    float4x4 modelMatrix;       // Object -> World
    float4x4 viewMatrix;        // World -> Camera
    float4x4 projectionMatrix;  // Camera -> Clip (perspective)
};

vertex VertexOut basicVertex(
    VertexIn in [[stage_in]],
    constant Uniforms &uniforms [[buffer(1)]])
{
    VertexOut out;

    // Transform position through the full matrix chain
    float4 worldPos = uniforms.modelMatrix * float4(in.position, 1.0);
    out.worldPosition = worldPos.xyz;
    out.position = uniforms.projectionMatrix * uniforms.viewMatrix * worldPos;

    // Transform normal (use inverse transpose of model matrix for correct normals)
    out.normal = (uniforms.modelMatrix * float4(in.normal, 0.0)).xyz;

    // Pass through texture coordinates
    out.texCoord = in.texCoord;

    return out;
}

    Coordinate Spaces

    MODEL SPACE (Object Space)
  Vertex positions relative to object center
  e.g., a cube from (-1,-1,-1) to (1,1,1)
         |
         | Model Matrix (translate, rotate, scale)
         v
WORLD SPACE
  Position in the scene
  e.g., cube at position (5, 0, -3)
         |
         | View Matrix (camera transform)
         v
VIEW SPACE (Camera Space / Eye Space)
  Position relative to camera
  Camera is at origin, looking down -Z
         |
         | Projection Matrix (perspective or orthographic)
         v
CLIP SPACE
  Normalized coordinates for GPU clipping
  X, Y in [-1, 1], Z in [0, 1]
         |
         | Perspective Divide (hardware, automatic)
         v
NDC (Normalized Device Coordinates)
  After dividing by W component
         |
         | Viewport Transform (hardware, automatic)
         v
SCREEN SPACE (Pixel Coordinates)
  Final pixel positions on screen

    Vertex Shader Animations

    Wobble / Wave Deformation

    vertex VertexOut wobbleVertex(
    VertexIn in [[stage_in]],
    constant Uniforms &uniforms [[buffer(1)]],
    constant float &time [[buffer(2)]])
{
    VertexOut out;

    // Displace Y position based on X position and time
    float3 pos = in.position;
    pos.y += sin(pos.x * 5.0 + time * 3.0) * 0.1;
    pos.y += cos(pos.z * 3.0 + time * 2.0) * 0.05;

    float4 worldPos = uniforms.modelMatrix * float4(pos, 1.0);
    out.position = uniforms.projectionMatrix * uniforms.viewMatrix * worldPos;
    out.texCoord = in.texCoord;
    out.normal = in.normal;
    out.worldPosition = worldPos.xyz;

    return out;
}

    Explode Effect

    vertex VertexOut explodeVertex(
    VertexIn in [[stage_in]],
    constant Uniforms &uniforms [[buffer(1)]],
    constant float &progress [[buffer(2)]])  // 0 = normal, 1 = fully exploded
{
    VertexOut out;

    // Push vertices outward along their normal
    float3 pos = in.position + in.normal * progress * 2.0;

    // Add some randomness based on position
    float hash = fract(sin(dot(in.position.xy, float2(12.9898, 78.233))) * 43758.5453);
    pos += in.normal * hash * progress * 0.5;

    float4 worldPos = uniforms.modelMatrix * float4(pos, 1.0);
    out.position = uniforms.projectionMatrix * uniforms.viewMatrix * worldPos;
    out.texCoord = in.texCoord;
    out.normal = in.normal;
    out.worldPosition = worldPos.xyz;

    return out;
}

    Scale Bounce

    vertex VertexOut bounceVertex(
    VertexIn in [[stage_in]],
    constant Uniforms &uniforms [[buffer(1)]],
    constant float &time [[buffer(2)]])
{
    VertexOut out;

    // Bounce scale using damped spring formula
    float t = fract(time * 0.5);
    float bounce = 1.0 + exp(-t * 5.0) * sin(t * 20.0) * 0.3;
    float3 pos = in.position * bounce;

    float4 worldPos = uniforms.modelMatrix * float4(pos, 1.0);
    out.position = uniforms.projectionMatrix * uniforms.viewMatrix * worldPos;
    out.texCoord = in.texCoord;
    out.normal = in.normal;
    out.worldPosition = worldPos.xyz;

    return out;
}

    Full-Screen Quad (For Post-Processing)

    For 2D effects, you don't need a vertex buffer. Use the full-screen triangle:

    // No vertex buffer needed — generates a full-screen triangle from vertex ID
vertex VertexOut fullscreenVertex(uint vertexID [[vertex_id]]) {
    VertexOut out;
    out.texCoord = float2((vertexID << 1) & 2, vertexID & 2);
    out.position = float4(out.texCoord * float2(2, -2) + float2(-1, 1), 0, 1);
    return out;
}

// Swift side:
encoder.drawPrimitives(type: .triangle, vertexStart: 0, vertexCount: 3)
// No vertex buffer set — vertexID generates the geometry

    Setting Up Vertex Descriptors (Swift Side)
