class Renderer: NSObject, MTKViewDelegate {
    let device: MTLDevice
    let commandQueue: MTLCommandQueue
    let pipelineState: MTLRenderPipelineState
    var time: Float = 0

    init(metalView: MTKView) {
        device = metalView.device!
        commandQueue = device.makeCommandQueue()!

        let library = device.makeDefaultLibrary()!
        let descriptor = MTLRenderPipelineDescriptor()
        descriptor.vertexFunction = library.makeFunction(name: "fullscreenVertex")
        descriptor.fragmentFunction = library.makeFunction(name: "myEffect")
        descriptor.colorAttachments[0].pixelFormat = metalView.colorPixelFormat

        pipelineState = try! device.makeRenderPipelineState(descriptor: descriptor)

        super.init()
        metalView.delegate = self
    }

    func draw(in view: MTKView) {
        time += 1.0 / Float(view.preferredFramesPerSecond)

        guard let drawable = view.currentDrawable,
              let descriptor = view.currentRenderPassDescriptor else { return }

        let buffer = commandQueue.makeCommandBuffer()!
        let encoder = buffer.makeRenderCommandEncoder(descriptor: descriptor)!

        encoder.setRenderPipelineState(pipelineState)
        encoder.setFragmentBytes(&time, length: 4, index: 0)
        encoder.drawPrimitives(type: .triangle, vertexStart: 0, vertexCount: 3)

        encoder.endEncoding()
        buffer.present(drawable)
        buffer.commit()
    }

    func mtkView(_ view: MTKView, drawableSizeWillChange size: CGSize) {}
}

    Option 2: CAMetalLayer (More Control)

    For when you need Metal rendering in a regular UIView without MTKView:

    class MetalLayerView: UIView {
    override class var layerClass: AnyClass { CAMetalLayer.self }

    var metalLayer: CAMetalLayer { layer as! CAMetalLayer }
    var device: MTLDevice!
    var commandQueue: MTLCommandQueue!
    var displayLink: CADisplayLink!

    func setup() {
        device = MTLCreateSystemDefaultDevice()!
        metalLayer.device = device
        metalLayer.pixelFormat = .bgra8Unorm_srgb
        metalLayer.framebufferOnly = true
        metalLayer.contentsScale = UIScreen.main.scale

        commandQueue = device.makeCommandQueue()!

        displayLink = CADisplayLink(target: self, selector: #selector(render))
        displayLink.preferredFrameRateRange = CAFrameRateRange(minimum: 60, maximum: 120)
        displayLink.add(to: .main, forMode: .common)
    }

    @objc func render() {
        guard let drawable = metalLayer.nextDrawable() else { return }
        // Render to drawable.texture, then present
    }
}

    Multi-Pass Rendering (Blur Pipeline)

    func renderBlur(source: MTLTexture, output: MTLTexture) {
    // Create intermediate texture
    let desc = MTLTextureDescriptor.texture2DDescriptor(
        pixelFormat: source.pixelFormat,
        width: source.width, height: source.height, mipmapped: false)
    desc.usage = [.shaderRead, .renderTarget]
    let intermediate = device.makeTexture(descriptor: desc)!

    let buffer = commandQueue.makeCommandBuffer()!

    // Pass 1: Horizontal blur (source -> intermediate)
    let pass1Desc = MTLRenderPassDescriptor()
    pass1Desc.colorAttachments[0].texture = intermediate
    pass1Desc.colorAttachments[0].loadAction = .dontCare
    pass1Desc.colorAttachments[0].storeAction = .store

    let enc1 = buffer.makeRenderCommandEncoder(descriptor: pass1Desc)!
    enc1.setRenderPipelineState(blurHPipeline)
    enc1.setFragmentTexture(source, index: 0)
    var dir = SIMD2<Float>(1, 0)
    enc1.setFragmentBytes(&dir, length: 8, index: 0)
    enc1.drawPrimitives(type: .triangle, vertexStart: 0, vertexCount: 3)
    enc1.endEncoding()

    // Pass 2: Vertical blur (intermediate -> output)
    let pass2Desc = MTLRenderPassDescriptor()
    pass2Desc.colorAttachments[0].texture = output
    pass2Desc.colorAttachments[0].loadAction = .dontCare
    pass2Desc.colorAttachments[0].storeAction = .store

    let enc2 = buffer.makeRenderCommandEncoder(descriptor: pass2Desc)!
    enc2.setRenderPipelineState(blurVPipeline)
    enc2.setFragmentTexture(intermediate, index: 0)
    dir = SIMD2<Float>(0, 1)
    enc2.setFragmentBytes(&dir, length: 8, index: 0)
    enc2.drawPrimitives(type: .triangle, vertexStart: 0, vertexCount: 3)
    enc2.endEncoding()

    buffer.commit()
}

    Rendering to Texture (Off-Screen)

    func renderToTexture() -> MTLTexture {
    let desc = MTLTextureDescriptor.texture2DDescriptor(
        pixelFormat: .rgba8Unorm, width: 512, height: 512, mipmapped: false)
    desc.usage = [.shaderRead, .renderTarget]
    let texture = device.makeTexture(descriptor: desc)!

    let passDesc = MTLRenderPassDescriptor()
    passDesc.colorAttachments[0].texture = texture
    passDesc.colorAttachments[0].loadAction = .clear
    passDesc.colorAttachments[0].clearColor = MTLClearColor(red: 0, green: 0, blue: 0, alpha: 0)
    passDesc.colorAttachments[0].storeAction = .store

    let buffer = commandQueue.makeCommandBuffer()!
    let encoder = buffer.makeRenderCommandEncoder(descriptor: passDesc)!
    // Draw your content...
    encoder.endEncoding()
    buffer.commit()
    buffer.waitUntilCompleted()

    return texture
}

    Capturing Screen Content for Processing

    // Snapshot a UIView to a Metal texture
func captureView(_ view: UIView) -> MTLTexture {
    let renderer = UIGraphicsImageRenderer(bounds: view.bounds)
    let image = renderer.image { ctx in
        view.drawHierarchy(in: view.bounds, afterScreenUpdates: false)
    }

    let loader = MTKTextureLoader(device: device)
    return try! loader.newTexture(cgImage: image.cgImage!, options: [
        .textureUsage: MTLTextureUsage.shaderRead.rawValue,
        .SRGB: false
    ])
}

    Checkpoint

    • [x] MTKView: easiest setup, handles display link and drawables
    • [x] CAMetalLayer: more control, embed Metal in any UIView
    • [x] Multi-pass rendering for effects like blur (ping-pong textures)
    • [x] Off-screen rendering to textures
    • [x] Capturing UIView content into Metal textures

    The foundation

    • MTKView = a ready-made canvas that handles the per-frame heartbeat and hands you a blank plate to draw on.
    • CAMetalLayer = the same canvas with manual controls, so you can drop Metal into any plain UIView and drive the timing yourself.
    • command queue / command buffer = the order rail and the order slip; you fill a slip with GPU instructions each frame and commit it.
    • drawable = the actual on-screen plate the GPU paints into, then presents so the user sees it.
    • render to texture = pointing the GPU's output at an off-screen image instead of the screen, so you can reuse the result later.
    • multi-pass (ping-pong) = running the effect in stages, feeding one pass's texture into the next — how a separable blur (horizontal then vertical) is built.
    • framebufferOnly = a promise that you won't read the pixels back, which lets Metal skip work and run faster.
    Prev← 16 — Blending and CompositingNext18 — Metal Shaders in SwiftUI (iOS 17+) →

    18 — Metal Shaders in SwiftUI (iOS 17+)

    Start here — the plain idea

    In UIKit you had to build the whole kitchen yourself. SwiftUI hands you a shortcut: take any view you already drew — text, an image, a whole scroll view — and run a tiny GPU program over its pixels with one modifier. The [[ stitchable ]] keyword is just a label that says "this shader is allowed to be plugged into SwiftUI's own pipeline," the same way throws labels a function.

    There are three flavors, and the difference is simply what your shader is allowed to touch. A colorEffect gets one pixel's color and can change that color — recolor, tint, grayscale — but it can't peek at the neighbors. A distortionEffect can't change colors at all; instead it answers a different question: "for the pixel landing here, where should I go grab its color from?" Move that lookup around and you get ripples, pixelation, fisheye. A layerEffect is the powerful one — it hands you the whole rendered view as something you can sample anywhere, so you can read many neighboring pixels and average them. That's what blur, glow, and edge detection need.

    maxSampleOffset is just you telling SwiftUI "my shader might reach this far for a pixel," so it knows how big a region to keep around. Too small and the edges get clipped; too big and you pay for area you never use.

    iOS 17 introduced [[ stitchable ]] shaders — the ability to apply custom Metal shaders directly to SwiftUI views. This is the most practical way to use Metal for visual effects in modern iOS apps.

    Three Effect Types

    // 1. colorEffect — per-pixel color transformation
view.colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.myEffect(.float(time)))

// 2. distortionEffect — per-pixel position displacement
view.distortionEffect(ShaderLibrary.myDistortion(.float(amount)),
                       maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 50, height: 50))

// 3. layerEffect — full texture access (sampling at arbitrary positions)
view.layerEffect(ShaderLibrary.myBlur(.float(radius)),
                  maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 64, height: 64))

    Color Effects

    Receives the current pixel color and position. Returns a new color. Cannot read neighboring pixels.

    Metal Shader

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

// MUST have [[ stitchable ]] attribute
// First param: position, second param: current color
// Additional params: your custom data

[[ stitchable ]] half4 grayscale(float2 position, half4 color) {
    half luma = dot(color.rgb, half3(0.2126, 0.7152, 0.0722));
    return half4(luma, luma, luma, color.a);
}

[[ stitchable ]] half4 tint(float2 position, half4 color, half4 tintColor) {
    return color * tintColor;
}

[[ stitchable ]] half4 wave(float2 position, half4 color, float time) {
    float brightness = sin(position.x * 0.05 + time * 3.0) * 0.5 + 0.5;
    return half4(color.rgb * half(brightness), color.a);
}

    SwiftUI Usage

    Text("Hello, Metal!")
    .font(.largeTitle)
    .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.grayscale())

Image("photo")
    .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.tint(.color(.red)))

Text("Waves")
    .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.wave(.float(elapsedTime)))

    Distortion Effects

    Receives a position, returns a new position to sample from. Shifts where each pixel reads its color from. Cannot change colors, only positions.

    Metal Shader

    // Returns a float2 — the new sample position
[[ stitchable ]] float2 ripple(float2 position, float2 origin, float time,
                              float amplitude, float frequency, float decay) {
    float dist = distance(position, origin);
    float wave = sin(dist * frequency - time * 10.0);
    float falloff = exp(-dist * decay) * exp(-time * 2.0);
    float2 direction = normalize(position - origin + 0.001);

    return position + direction * wave * amplitude * falloff;
}

[[ stitchable ]] float2 pixelate(float2 position, float size) {
    return floor(position / size) * size + size * 0.5;
}

[[ stitchable ]] float2 fisheye(float2 position, float2 center, float radius, float strength) {
    float2 diff = position - center;
    float dist = length(diff);

    if (dist < radius) {
        float percent = dist / radius;
        float distortion = pow(percent, strength);
        return center + diff * distortion / percent;
    }
    return position;
}

    SwiftUI Usage

    Image("photo")
    .distortionEffect(
        ShaderLibrary.ripple(
            .float2(tapLocation),
            .float(elapsedTime),
            .float(12),    // amplitude
            .float(15),    // frequency
            .float(0.02)   // decay
        ),
        maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 30, height: 30)
    )

Text("Pixelated")
    .distortionEffect(
        ShaderLibrary.pixelate(.float(10)),
        maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 10, height: 10)
    )

    maxSampleOffset: Tells SwiftUI the maximum distance (in points) a pixel might read from. Set it to at least your maximum distortion amount. Too small = clipping artifacts. Too large = wasted performance.

    Layer Effects

    The most powerful type. Receives the view as a SwiftUI::Layer that you can sample at any position. This enables blur, glow, edge detection, and any effect that reads neighboring pixels.

    Metal Shader

    // The Layer parameter gives you access to the full rendered view
[[ stitchable ]] half4 simpleBlur(float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer, float radius) {
    half4 sum = half4(0);
    float count = 0;

    for (float y = -radius; y <= radius; y += 1.0) {
        for (float x = -radius; x <= radius; x += 1.0) {
            sum += layer.sample(position + float2(x, y));
            count += 1.0;
        }
    }

    return sum / half(count);
}

[[ stitchable ]] half4 glow(float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer, float radius, float intensity) {
    half4 original = layer.sample(position);
    half4 blurred = half4(0);
    float count = 0;

    for (float y = -radius; y <= radius; y += 2.0) {
        for (float x = -radius; x <= radius; x += 2.0) {
            blurred += layer.sample(position + float2(x, y));
            count += 1.0;
        }
    }
    blurred /= half(count);

    // Add glow (blurred version on top of original)
    return original + blurred * half(intensity);
}

[[ stitchable ]] half4 progressiveBlur(float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer,
                                      float2 size, float maxRadius) {
    // Blur increases from top to bottom
    float progress = position.y / size.y;
    float radius = progress * maxRadius;

    if (radius < 1.0) return layer.sample(position);

    half4 sum = half4(0);
    float totalWeight = 0;
    int r = int(min(radius, 20.0));  // Cap for performance

    for (int y = -r; y <= r; y++) {
        for (int x = -r; x <= r; x++) {
            float weight = exp(-float(x*x + y*y) / (2.0 * radius * radius));
            sum += layer.sample(position + float2(x, y)) * half(weight);
            totalWeight += weight;
        }
    }

    return sum / half(totalWeight);
}

    SwiftUI Usage

    ScrollView {
    VStack { /* content */ }
}
.layerEffect(
    ShaderLibrary.progressiveBlur(
        .float2(CGSize(width: screenWidth, height: screenHeight)),
        .float(15)  // maxRadius
    ),
    maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 20, height: 20)
)

Text("Glowing")
    .font(.system(size: 60, weight: .bold))
    .layerEffect(
        ShaderLibrary.glow(.float(8), .float(0.5)),
        maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 8, height: 8)
    )

    Passing Data Types

    // Float
.float(3.14)

// Float2 (from CGPoint or CGSize)
.float2(CGPoint(x: 100, y: 200))
.float2(CGSize(width: 400, height: 800))

// Color
.color(.red)
.color(Color(uiColor: myColor))

// Image (as texture)
.image(Image("myTexture"))

// Data (raw bytes)
.data(myData)

// FloatArray (for passing multiple values)
// Not directly available — use multiple .float() params

    Animating Shaders

    struct AnimatedShaderView: View {
    let startDate = Date()

    var body: some View {
        TimelineView(.animation) { context in
            let elapsed = context.date.timeIntervalSince(startDate)

            Image("photo")
                .colorEffect(
                    ShaderLibrary.wave(.float(Float(elapsed)))
                )
        }
    }
}

    With Gestures

    struct InteractiveShaderView: View {
    @State private var tapLocation: CGPoint = .zero
    @State private var tapTime: Date = .distantPast

    var body: some View {
        TimelineView(.animation) { context in
            let elapsed = context.date.timeIntervalSince(tapTime)

            Image("photo")
                .distortionEffect(
                    ShaderLibrary.ripple(
                        .float2(tapLocation),
                        .float(Float(elapsed)),
                        .float(12),
                        .float(15),
                        .float(0.02)
                    ),
                    maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 30, height: 30)
                )
                .onTapGesture { location in
                    tapLocation = location
                    tapTime = .now
                }
        }
    }
}

    .visualEffect (iOS 17+) — Position-Based Effects Without Metal

    For simpler effects that don't need custom shaders:

    ScrollView {
    ForEach(items) { item in
        ItemRow(item: item)
            .visualEffect { content, proxy in
                let frame = proxy.frame(in: .scrollView)
                let distance = abs(frame.midY - UIScreen.main.bounds.midY)
                let normalizedDistance = min(distance / 300, 1.0)

                content
                    .blur(radius: normalizedDistance * 10)
                    .opacity(1.0 - normalizedDistance * 0.5)
                    .scaleEffect(1.0 - normalizedDistance * 0.1)
            }
    }
}

    Combining with SwiftUI Transitions

    struct ShaderTransition: View {
    @State private var isShowing = false

    var body: some View {
        if isShowing {
            ContentView()
                .transition(.identity.combined(with:
                    .modifier(
                        active: ShaderModifier(progress: 0),
                        identity: ShaderModifier(progress: 1)
                    )
                ))
        }
    }
}

struct ShaderModifier: ViewModifier {
    var progress: Float

    func body(content: Content) -> some View {
        content
            .layerEffect(
                ShaderLibrary.dissolve(.float(progress)),
                maxSampleOffset: .zero
            )
    }
}

    Performance Tips

    1. Keep maxSampleOffset tight — larger values = more processing
    2. Use half4 return type — 2x faster on Apple GPUs
    3. Cap loop iterations — min(radius, 20.0) prevents GPU timeout
    4. Prefer colorEffect over layerEffect when you don't need neighbor reads
    5. Avoid layerEffect on large views with high sample offsets — it scales with view size x offset area

    Exercises

    1. Chromatic aberration: Write a layerEffect that splits R, G, B channels with slight offsets.

    2. Dissolve transition: Write a colorEffect that uses noise to dissolve a view (pixels randomly become transparent based on a progress value).

    3. Magnifier: Write a layerEffect that magnifies the area under a touch point.

    4. Scroll blur: Apply progressive blur to a ScrollView where items blur as they approach the edges.

    Checkpoint

    Before moving on, you should understand: - [x] [[ stitchable ]] attribute makes shaders usable in SwiftUI - [x] Three types: colorEffect (color only), distortionEffect (position only), layerEffect (full access) - [x] Function signatures: position + color/layer + custom params - [x] Passing data: .float(), .float2(), .color(), .image() - [x] maxSampleOffset controls how far a shader can read from neighboring pixels - [x] TimelineView(.animation) for shader animation - [x] SwiftUI::Layer provides .sample(position) in layer effects

    The foundation

    • [[ stitchable ]] = a label saying "this shader may be plugged into SwiftUI," just like throws labels a function.
    • colorEffect = recolors one pixel at a time; sees its own color, never the neighbors.
    • distortionEffect = leaves colors alone and instead moves where each pixel reads from — bend that lookup and you get ripples or fisheye.
    • layerEffect = the full-access one; hands you the whole view to sample anywhere, which is what blur and glow need.
    • maxSampleOffset = how far out your shader is allowed to reach for a pixel — set it to your real reach, no more.
    • SwiftUI::Layer.sample(position) = "give me the color at this spot" — the tool that lets a layer effect read its neighbors.
    • TimelineView(.animation) = a clock that re-renders every frame so you can feed elapsed time into a shader and make it move.
    Prev← 17 — Metal in UIKitNext18a — Feeding Data In: Time, Size, Colors & Uniforms →

    18a — Feeding Data In: Time, Size, Colors & Uniforms

    Start here — the plain idea

    A shader is a pure function: it runs on the GPU, sees only the arguments you hand it, and returns a color. It can't reach into your app to read the clock, the user's finger, or a slider. So the entire art of "making a shader do something" is feeding it the right data every frame. This lesson is the catalog of what you can pass from SwiftUI and how.

    Think of the shader as a vending machine. It already knows two things for free — position (which pixel) and color (that pixel's current color). Everything else is a coin you drop in the slot: the current time, the view's size, a tint color, a texture, a touch point. Drop the right coins and the machine produces the effect. The order of the coins matters, and each has a specific shape.

    The whole flow is one-way: SwiftUI → GPU, argument by argument, every frame. Get comfortable with this menu and you can drive any shader.

    The menu: argument types you can pass

    In Swift you build a shader call like ShaderLibrary.myShader(arg, arg, …). Each arg is a typed helper that matches one parameter in the Metal function (after the required position/color):

    Swift helper Metal parameter it feeds Use it for
    .float(x) float x time, a slider, intensity, radius
    .float2(p) float2 p size, a point, a direction (from CGPoint/CGSize)
    .float3(_) / .float4(_) float3 / float4 raw vectors, a packed RGBA
    .color(c) half4 c a tint or theme color (Color → premapped)
    .image(img) texture2d<half> t a photo/gradient/mask to sample
    .floatArray([…]) constant float *a a handful of values (pass the count too)
    .data(d) constant T * a packed struct/array of bytes
    .boundingRect float4 rect the view's bounds — size without GeometryReader

    Two rules that never change: arguments are positional (the 1st .float2 fills the 1st float2 parameter after color), and types must line up exactly. There are no names to save you — a mismatch is garbage or a crash.

    Time — the "timeline" that drives animation

    The single most common thing you'll pass is elapsed seconds. SwiftUI's TimelineView(.animation) re-renders every frame and hands you the current date; subtract a start date to get seconds, and pass it as a .float:

    struct AnimatedView: View {
    let start = Date()
    var body: some View {
        TimelineView(.animation) { tl in                    // ticks ~60–120×/sec
            let t = Float(tl.date.timeIntervalSince(start))  // elapsed seconds
            Rectangle().colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.pulse(.float(t)))
        }
    }
}

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 pulse(float2 position, half4 color, float t) {
    float b = 0.5 + 0.5 * sin(t * 3.0);      // a value that breathes over time
    return half4(half3(b), 1.0h);
}

    TimelineView(.animation) is the timeline: it's the clock that keeps the shader moving. The multiplier on t (* 3.0) sets the speed, exactly like the sin math from the building blocks. (For a very long-running app you can wrap t with fmod(t, 6.2831) to keep the float small and precise — sin only cares about the angle mod 2π.)

    Size — so your shader is resolution-independent

    Because position arrives in points, you almost always need the view's size to compute uv = position / size. Two ways to get it:

    // A) GeometryReader — explicit
GeometryReader { geo in
    Rectangle().colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.myShader(.float2(geo.size)))
}

// B) .visualEffect — inline, no wrapper view
Rectangle().visualEffect { content, proxy in
    content.colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.myShader(.float2(proxy.size)))
}

    Or skip passing it at all and use .boundingRect inside the shader — SwiftUI fills a float4 with the view's rect (origin.xy, size.zw):

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 myShader(float2 position, half4 color, float4 bounds) {
    float2 uv = position / bounds.zw;   // .zw = width, height
    ...
}

    Colors, images, and bulk data

    Colors — pass a SwiftUI Color; it arrives as a half4 you can tint with:

    .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.tint(.color(.orange)))

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 tint(float2 position, half4 color, half4 tint) {
    return color * tint;                 // multiply the view's pixels by the tint
}

    Images/textures — hand over an Image and sample it inside the shader (great for masks, gradients, look-up tables):

    .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.overlay(.image(Image("grain"))))

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 overlay(float2 position, half4 color, texture2d<half> tex) {
    constexpr sampler s(filter::linear);
    half4 g = tex.sample(s, position / float2(tex.get_width(), tex.get_height()));
    return color * g;
}

    Several values at once — use .floatArray for a small set (and pass the count as a separate .float), or .data for a packed struct. For large or per-frame-changing arrays (thousands of particles), stitchable arguments aren't the right tool — that's when you drop to a full Metal buffer (10-buffers-and-uniforms).

    Putting it together: a little "control panel" shader

    Here's a shader fed time + size + two colors, with the Swift call and the Metal signature lined up so you can see every coin drop into its slot:

    TimelineView(.animation) { tl in
    let t = Float(tl.date.timeIntervalSinceReferenceDate)
    Rectangle().visualEffect { content, proxy in
        content.colorEffect(
            ShaderLibrary.bandsShader(
                .float(t),                 // → float time
                .float2(proxy.size),       // → float2 size
                .color(.blue),             // → half4 a
                .color(.pink)              // → half4 b
            )
        )
    }
}

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 bandsShader(float2 position, half4 color,
                                   float time, float2 size, half4 a, half4 b) {
    float2 uv = position / size;
    float wave = 0.5 + 0.5 * sin(uv.y * 20.0 + time * 2.0);  // moving bands
    return mix(a, b, half(wave));                            // blend the two colors
}

    Read the argument list top to bottom in both blocks — they're the same sequence. That one-to-one correspondence is the whole skill: decide what the effect needs, pass exactly those, in order.

    The foundation

    • shaders are pure functions = they know only what you pass; there's no global clock or touch they can read on their own.
    • argument helpers = .float, .float2, .color, .image, .floatArray, .data, .boundingRect — each maps to one Metal parameter, positionally.
    • time = TimelineView(.animation) → elapsed seconds → .float(t); this is the "timeline" that animates a shader.
    • size = GeometryReader/.visualEffect proxy.size → .float2, or .boundingRect inside the shader, so you can compute uv.
    • color / image = pass a Color (arrives as half4) or an Image (a texture2d you sample).
    • bulk data = .floatArray/.data for a few values; a real MTLBuffer (10-buffers-and-uniforms) for big/per-frame arrays.
    • it's one-way = Swift feeds the GPU every frame; the shader only returns a color (see 18b-gestures-to-shaders for the interaction loop).

    Related

    • The three effect types this builds on: 18-metal-in-swiftui
    • Map user input into these arguments: 18b-gestures-to-shaders
    • Animating with the time you pass: 23-animation-in-shaders
    • Low-level buffers under the hood: 10-buffers-and-uniforms

    18b — Gestures → Shaders: Tap, Drag, Pinch, Swipe

    Start here — the plain idea

    Now the fun part: making the shader react to the user. Tap and a ripple spreads from your finger; pinch and the image bulges; drag and the light follows. The trick is always the same three-step loop, and once you see it you can wire any gesture to any effect.

    The loop is: a gesture updates some SwiftUI @State → that state is passed to the shader as a uniform → the shader redraws using it. The finger doesn't talk to the GPU directly; it talks to your @State, and your @State is what you feed the shader every frame. SwiftUI re-renders whenever the state changes, so the effect tracks the gesture in real time.

    One honest thing up front, since it clears up a common confusion: a fragment shader can't "write back" to your app. It only ever returns a color. So the source of truth (where the finger is, how zoomed you are) lives in Swift @State; the shader is a fast, stateless renderer of that truth. When you want motion to continue after the finger lifts — momentum, a settling ripple — you keep a timeline running and let the numbers evolve in Swift or in the shader's own math. (For true GPU-side state that persists — particle systems, fluid — you need compute shaders with ping-pong buffers; see 08-compute-shaders and 10-buffers-and-uniforms.)

    The universal pattern (memorize this)

    Every gesture-driven shader is these three steps:

    1. Capture the gesture into @State (a point, a scale, an angle, a velocity).
    2. Normalize it into the shader's world — points → uv (divide by size), or clamp a scale into a sane range.
    3. Pass & map it as a uniform, then use remap/smoothstep inside the shader to turn the raw input into the effect's range.

    Step 3 is pure Choosing the Math: "as the pinch goes 1→3, make the bulge go 0→0.5." The shader is just the place you apply that mapping.

    Tap — a ripple from where you touched

    Capture the tap location, remember when it happened, and let a TimelineView animate the elapsed time so the ripple expands and fades:

    struct TapRipple: View {
    @State private var center: CGPoint = .zero
    @State private var tapTime: Date = .distantPast

    var body: some View {
        TimelineView(.animation) { tl in
            let elapsed = Float(tl.date.timeIntervalSince(tapTime))
            Image("photo").resizable().scaledToFill()
                .visualEffect { content, proxy in
                    content.distortionEffect(
                        ShaderLibrary.ripple(
                            .float2(center),   // where the finger landed (points)
                            .float(elapsed)    // seconds since the tap
                        ),
                        maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 30, height: 30)
                    )
                }
                .onTapGesture { location in     // ← capture step
                    center = location
                    tapTime = .now
                }
        }
    }
}

    [[ stitchable ]] float2 ripple(float2 position, float2 center, float t) {
    float d = distance(position, center);                 // pixels from the touch
    float wave = sin(d * 0.08 - t * 12.0);                // outgoing ring
    float falloff = exp(-d * 0.01) * exp(-t * 3.0);       // fade with distance + time
    float2 dir = normalize(position - center + 1e-4);
    return position + dir * wave * 12.0 * falloff;        // shove the sample point
}

    This is a distortionEffect, so it returns a position (where to read the pixel from), not a color — bending the lookup around the touch makes the image wobble. Note the loop: onTapGesture writes @State, the TimelineView re-feeds elapsed each frame, and the ripple animates itself out.

    Drag — the effect follows your finger

    DragGesture gives you a live location (and translation, and even velocity). Store it and normalize to uv:

    struct DragLight: View {
    @State private var finger: CGPoint = .init(x: 150, y: 150)
    var body: some View {
        Rectangle().visualEffect { content, proxy in
            content.colorEffect(
                ShaderLibrary.spotlight(.float2(finger), .float2(proxy.size))
            )
        }
        .gesture(
            DragGesture(minimumDistance: 0)          // 0 = also fire on touch-down
                .onChanged { finger = $0.location }   // ← capture step
        )
    }
}

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 spotlight(float2 position, half4 color, float2 finger, float2 size) {
    float d = distance(position / size, finger / size);   // normalized distance
    float glow = smoothstep(0.4, 0.0, d);                 // bright near the finger
    return color + half4(half3(glow) * 0.6h, 0.0h);
}

    Same three steps: capture location → normalize by size → map distance to brightness with smoothstep. Swap smoothstep bounds to tighten or spread the light.

    Pinch — scale a bulge or zoom

    MagnifyGesture (iOS 17+) reports a magnification value (1.0 = no zoom). Clamp it to a sane range and feed it in:

    @State private var zoom: CGFloat = 1
// …
.gesture(
    MagnifyGesture()
        .onChanged { zoom = min(max($0.magnification, 1), 3) }   // clamp 1×–3×
)
.visualEffect { content, proxy in
    content.distortionEffect(
        ShaderLibrary.fisheye(.float2(proxy.size), .float(Float(zoom))),
        maxSampleOffset: CGSize(width: 60, height: 60)
    )
}

    [[ stitchable ]] float2 fisheye(float2 position, float2 size, float zoom) {
    float2 c = size * 0.5;
    float2 v = position - c;
    float r = length(v) / length(c);            // 0 at center → 1 at corner
    float strength = (zoom - 1.0);              // 0 when not pinched
    float k = 1.0 - strength * (1.0 - r);       // bulge more near center
    return c + v * k;
}

    zoom arrives already clamped from Swift (never below 1, never past 3), so the shader math stays stable — clamping in @State is doing the same protective job as clamp in a shader.

    Rotation & swipe (velocity)

    Rotation — RotateGesture (iOS 17+) gives an Angle; pass .float(Float(angle.radians)) and spin uv with a 2×2 rotation inside the shader (float2(u*cosθ - v*sinθ, u*sinθ + v*cosθ)).

    Swipe / flick — a swipe is just a DragGesture you read .velocity from in onEnded. Feed the release velocity in, then let a TimelineView decay it so the effect coasts and settles — the spring/momentum idea from springs & easing:

    .gesture(
    DragGesture()
        .onEnded { value in
            velocity = value.velocity.width      // points/sec at release
        }
)
// in a TimelineView, each frame: velocity *= 0.92 (decay), feed it to the shader

    Combining gestures — pan and pinch at once with SimultaneousGesture(dragGesture, magnifyGesture) (or .gesture stacked), each writing its own @State. The shader just receives both uniforms.

    Try it live

    The demo above is the whole idea in miniature: it feeds u_time and u_mouse (your pointer, normalized 0–1) into a GLSL shader running on your GPU. Move the mouse over the canvas and watch the effect track it — that's exactly @State → uniform → redraw, just in the browser. Edit the code and hit run to map the mouse to something new.

    The mapping is the real work (not the plumbing)

    Capturing a gesture is easy; making it feel right is the craft, and it's the interaction-math you already know:

    • remap(input, a, b, c, d) — turn the gesture's natural range into the effect's range. "Drag 0→300pt ⇒ blur 0→20."
    • smoothstep — soften the ends so the effect eases in instead of snapping.
    • clamp / clamp-in-@State — keep values in bounds (pinch can't zoom to 0×).
    • decay / spring in a TimelineView — carry momentum after the finger lifts.

    The shader is where you apply the mapping; the decision of which mapping is Choosing the Math all over again.

    The foundation

    • the interaction loop = gesture → @State → shader uniform → redraw; SwiftUI re-renders on state change so the effect tracks the finger.
    • capture / normalize / map = the three steps behind every gesture-driven shader.
    • tap = onTapGesture { location } + a tapTime, animated by TimelineView for an expanding ripple (a distortionEffect).
    • drag = DragGesture().onChanged { $0.location }, normalized by size, mapped with smoothstep.
    • pinch = MagnifyGesture magnification, clamped in @State, fed as a .float to a fisheye/bulge.
    • swipe = DragGesture .velocity in onEnded, then decayed in a TimelineView for momentum.
    • shaders don't write back = they only return a color; the source of truth stays in @State. True GPU-side feedback needs compute + ping-pong buffers (08-compute-shaders).
    • feel = mapping = remap/smoothstep/clamp decide how raw input becomes the effect — pure Choosing the Math.

    Related

    • What you're passing in: 18a-feeding-data-into-shaders
    • The effect types you're driving: 18-metal-in-swiftui
    • Momentum & settling after a flick: Springs & Easing
    • True GPU-side state (particles, fluid): 08-compute-shaders
