    29 — The SDF Toolkit: Shapes, Smooth-Min & Soft Particles

    Start here — the plain idea

    Imagine standing anywhere on a map and, instead of coordinates, being handed a single number: how far is the nearest coastline? Stand on land and it's positive, wade into the sea and it flips negative, and right on the water's edge it reads zero. That number, computed for every pixel at once, is a signed distance field (SDF). It doesn't draw a shape — it describes the distance to a shape's surface, with a sign that tells you which side you're on.

    Once you have that "how far to the edge" map, drawing is easy. Feed the distance into smoothstep and you turn distance into ink: black where you're deep inside, fading to nothing just outside the edge. Want two shapes at once? Take the min of their distances — the closer surface wins, and you get their union for free.

    The magic trick is smooth-min. Ordinary min gives a hard crease where two shapes meet. Smooth-min rounds that crease off, so two circles flow together like two blobs of mercury pulling into one. That single function is what makes procedural smoke, lava lamps, and gooey blobs look organic instead of geometric. We built the first SDFs back in 04-coordinates-and-color; this lesson turns them into a real toolkit.

    The two building-block SDFs

    Every SDF is a function from a point p to a distance. Here are the two you'll reuse constantly.

    float sdCircle(float2 p, float r){ return length(p) - r; }
float sdBox(float2 p, float2 b){ float2 d = abs(p) - b; return length(max(d, 0.0)) + min(max(d.x, d.y), 0.0); }

    sdCircle is the whole idea in one line. length(p) is the distance from the origin to p; subtracting the radius r shifts the "zero" out to the circle's rim. Inside the circle length(p) < r, so the result is negative; outside it's positive; exactly on the rim it's zero. To move the circle, subtract a center first: sdCircle(p - center, r).

    sdBox is trickier because a box has flat sides and sharp corners. abs(p) folds all four quadrants into one, so we only reason about the top-right corner. d = abs(p) - b measures how far past the box's half-extents b we are on each axis (negative means still inside on that axis). The length(max(d, 0.0)) term handles the outside: clamping negatives to zero means only the axes we've actually exceeded contribute, giving true straight-line distance to the nearest edge or corner. The min(max(d.x, d.y), 0.0) term handles the inside: when both d components are negative, max(d.x, d.y) is the least-negative one — the distance to the closest wall — and min(..., 0.0) keeps it only when we're inside. Together they give a correct signed distance everywhere.

    Turning distance into a drawn shape

    A distance field is invisible until you map it to brightness. smoothstep is the standard tool.

    float d = sdCircle(p - center, 0.3);
float m = smoothstep(0.0, edge, -d);   // 1 inside, 0 outside, soft ramp of width `edge`

    We negate d so that inside (negative distance) becomes positive. smoothstep(0.0, edge, -d) then reads 1 wherever -d is past edge (well inside the shape), 0 wherever -d is below 0 (outside the edge), and a smooth ramp of width edge across the boundary. Larger edge means a softer, blurrier rim.

    That soft rim is nice for glows, but for a crisp shape you want the edge to be exactly one pixel wide no matter how far you zoom. fwidth gives you that — it reports how fast d changes between neighboring pixels, i.e. the size of one pixel measured in distance units.

    float fw = fwidth(d);
float m = smoothstep(fw, -fw, d);   // anti-aliased edge, always ~1px wide

    Here the ramp goes from +fw (just outside) to -fw (just inside), so the transition straddles the true edge and spans a single pixel's worth of distance. Note the order — fw then -fw — which makes the shape 1 inside (small/negative d) and 0 outside. This is the reliable way to anti-alias any SDF without multisampling.

    Union, and the gooey smooth-union

    Two shapes combine by taking the closer surface, which is just min of their distances.

    float d = min(sdCircle(p - a, 0.3), sdCircle(p - b, 0.3));   // hard union

    This works, but where the two circles overlap you get a sharp crease — a kink in the field. To make them merge organically, replace min with the IQ polynomial smooth minimum.

    // IQ polynomial smooth minimum — gooey union, k = blend radius
float smin(float a, float b, float k){
    float h = clamp(0.5 + 0.5*(a-b)/k, 0.0, 1.0);
    return mix(a, b, h) - k*h*(1.0-h);
}

    Read it as a smarter min. h measures how close the two distances are, normalized by the blend radius k: when a and b are far apart, h saturates to 0 or 1 and mix(a, b, h) picks the smaller one — you get ordinary min. But when the two surfaces are within k of each other, h sits near 0.5 and the correction term -k*h*(1.0-h) subtracts a little extra distance, pulling the field below either shape and bulging out a smooth bridge between them. That subtracted bulge is the merging mercury. Bigger k means a wider, softer weld; k near zero collapses back to a hard min. Swap min for smin in the union above and the two circles flow into a peanut.

    Soft particles and glows

    Not everything needs a hard edge. Smoke motes, sparks, and bokeh are just soft round falloffs — brightest at a center, fading to nothing at some radius.

    // soft round particle / glow: 1 at the center, fading to 0 at radius r
float glow(float2 p, float2 center, float r){ return smoothstep(r, 0.0, length(p - center)); }

    length(p - center) is the distance from the particle's center. smoothstep(r, 0.0, dist) reverses the usual direction: it reads 1 when the distance is 0 (dead center) and ramps down to 0 at distance r. The result is a smooth circular blob you can add straight into your color.

    For an even softer, haloed falloff with no hard cutoff, use an exponential instead:

    float g = exp(-length(p - center) * k);   // never truly reaches 0; k controls tightness

    exp(-dist * k) starts at 1 and decays forever, so the glow has a bright core and an endless faint halo — great for stars and embers. Larger k tightens it; smaller k spreads a wide bloom.

    A field of soft particles, summed additively

    The reason glows are worth having is that they stack. Scatter several and add their contributions — light is additive, so overlapping glows brighten and bloom exactly like real light.

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 particleField(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size, float time) {
    float2 p = (position - 0.5*size) / size.y;   // centered, aspect-correct
    half3 col = half3(0.0h);
    for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) {
        float fi = float(i);
        float2 c = 0.5 * float2(sin(fi * 1.7 + time), cos(fi * 2.3 + time * 0.7));
        col += half3(glow(p, c, 0.25)) * 0.6h;   // additive accumulation
    }
    return half4(min(col, 1.0h), 1.0h);
}

    We center coordinates with (position - 0.5*size) / size.y so (0,0) is the middle and the aspect ratio is preserved. Each iteration places a glow at a slowly-orbiting center c driven by time, and col += sums them — where blobs overlap, brightness piles up. The final min(col, 1.0h) just keeps values from blowing past white. This additive-layering instinct is the whole basis of the next lesson: stack many offset fields and the eye reads volume and depth (30-layering-and-parallax).

    Brief SwiftUI usage, passing time for the drift:

    TimelineView(.animation) { ctx in
    let t = ctx.date.timeIntervalSinceReferenceDate
    Color.black
        .visualEffect { content, proxy in
            content.colorEffect(
                ShaderLibrary.particleField(.float2(proxy.size), .float(t))
            )
        }
}

    Shadertoy names map directly: iTime is our time, iResolution is our size.

    The foundation

    • signed distance field (SDF) = a per-pixel number giving distance to a shape's surface, negative inside and positive outside.
    • sdCircle / sdBox = the two primitive SDFs; translate by subtracting a center, and abs-folding lets a box reason about one corner.
    • smoothstep-to-ink = feeding -d into smoothstep turns distance into a filled shape with a soft rim.
    • fwidth anti-aliasing = smoothstep(fw, -fw, d) makes an edge exactly one pixel wide at any zoom.
    • union = min of two distances draws both shapes, but with a hard crease where they meet.
    • smooth-min (smin) = a polynomial blend that welds shapes together like merging mercury; k sets the weld radius.
    • glow = smoothstep(r, 0.0, length(p-c)) or exp(-dist*k) makes a soft round particle with a bright core.
    • additive layering = summing glows brightens overlaps like real light, and is the seed of depth and volume.

    Related

    04-coordinates-and-color · 30-layering-and-parallax · 26-advanced-noise-fbm

    Prev: 28-domain-warping · Next: 30-layering-and-parallax · Hub: 00-roadmap

    Prev← 28 — Domain Warping: Feeding Noise Into NoiseNext30 — Layering & Fake Parallax: Volumetric Depth Without 3D →

    30 — Layering & Fake Parallax: Volumetric Depth Without 3D

    Start here — the plain idea

    Look out a train window and something odd happens: the fence beside the track blurs past, the fields roll by at a walking pace, and the distant mountains barely move at all. Nothing changed about the scenery's flatness — it's the difference in speed that your brain reads as depth. Near things sweep fast, far things crawl. This is motion parallax, and it's the cheapest depth cue there is.

    An old theatre used the same trick with painted backdrop flats slid across the stage at different rates. Every flat is dead flat, yet slide the foreground bush faster than the painted hills behind it and the audience perceives a deep, layered world. No one built a 3D set.

    We can do exactly this in a shader. Take one particle or noise field, stamp out fifteen copies, and give each copy its own scale, drift speed, and brightness — big-fast-bright in front, small-slow-dim behind. Add them all up. The eye stitches the speed-and-size gradient into volume, and you get smoke, dust, or a deep starfield with zero real 3D. This is the "15 offset particle fields" technique, and it's the additive layering from 29-sdf-toolkit turned into depth.

    The whole pattern in one loop

    Here is the complete shader. Everything interesting happens inside the loop.

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 layeredField(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size, float time) {
    float2 uv = (position - 0.5*size) / size.y;
    half3 col = half3(0.0h);
    const int LAYERS = 15;
    for (int i = 0; i < LAYERS; i++) {
        float fi = float(i) / float(LAYERS);          // 0 (near) .. ~1 (far)
        float scale = mix(1.0, 3.0, fi);              // far layers tiled smaller
        float speed = mix(0.25, 0.05, fi);            // far layers drift slower
        float bright = mix(1.0, 0.25, fi);            // far layers dimmer
        float2 p = uv * scale + float2(fi * 13.7, time * speed);
        float spark = smoothstep(0.9, 1.0, fract(sin(dot(floor(p), float2(41.3, 289.1))) * 43758.5)); // cheap per-cell spark
        col += half3(spark) * half(bright);
    }
    return half4(col, 1.0h);
}

    We start with centered, aspect-correct coordinates: (position - 0.5*size) / size.y puts (0,0) in the middle and divides by height so the field isn't stretched on wide screens. col accumulates the result, and LAYERS is how many flats we're sliding.

    The depth ramp: fi

    float fi = float(i) / float(LAYERS);   // 0 (near) .. ~1 (far)

    fi is the single knob that decides how "far away" this layer is. For layer 0 it's 0 (nearest), climbing toward 1 for the last layer (farthest). Every per-layer property is just a mix driven by fi, so one number sweeps a whole layer from foreground to background.

    float scale = mix(1.0, 3.0, fi);    // far layers tiled smaller
float speed = mix(0.25, 0.05, fi);  // far layers drift slower
float bright = mix(1.0, 0.25, fi);  // far layers dimmer

    Read all three as the parallax rules made literal. Near layers (fi≈0) use scale 1, so features are large — like the fence right at the window. Far layers (fi≈1) use scale 3, tiling the field three times smaller so features look tiny and distant. speed runs the opposite way: 0.25 for near, 0.05 for far, so the foreground sweeps and the background crawls — the exact train-window gradient. bright fades from full to a quarter, because distant things are dimmer and hazier. These three gradients are the entire depth illusion.

    The sampling coordinate, and why offsets decorrelate

    float2 p = uv * scale + float2(fi * 13.7, time * speed);

    Two things are added to the scaled coordinate. time * speed is the drift — it scrolls this layer's field along Y at that layer's own pace, which is what your eye reads as motion. The fi * 13.7 term is a per-layer spatial offset: an arbitrary, irregular shove that lands each layer at a different place in the field. Without it, every layer would sample the same pattern at the same spot and they'd all light up in lockstep — you'd see one bright field, not fifteen independent ones. The offset decorrelates the layers so their sparks fall in different places and the accumulation looks like genuine scattered depth rather than a stack of identical stencils. The odd constant 13.7 is just there to be irrational-ish and avoid accidental alignment.

    The per-cell spark, and additive accumulation

    float spark = smoothstep(0.9, 1.0, fract(sin(dot(floor(p), float2(41.3, 289.1))) * 43758.5));

    This is a placeholder field, not the point of the lesson. floor(p) snaps to an integer grid cell; the sin(dot(...)) * 43758.5 then fract is the classic cheap hash, giving each cell a pseudo-random value in [0,1). smoothstep(0.9, 1.0, ...) keeps only the top tenth of cells, lighting a sparse scatter of bright points — a stand-in for stars or dust motes.

    col += half3(spark) * half(bright);

    Each layer's sparks, dimmed by its own bright, are added into col. Additive accumulation is what stacks the "volume": overlapping layers pile brightness the way real light and haze do, and the summed result reads as something with depth and density rather than a single flat plane.

    Swap the spark for a real field

    The spark line is deliberately crude so the structure stays visible. In practice you replace it with a proper field from earlier lessons — a Voronoi cell pattern, the soft particles/glows from 29-sdf-toolkit, or fbm noise from 26-advanced-noise-fbm:

    // float layerValue = fbm(p);                 // drifting smoke layers
// float layerValue = glow(p, cellPoint, r);  // soft particle layer
col += half3(layerValue) * half(bright);

    The loop, the fi ramp, and the additive sum are unchanged — only the thing being sampled differs. That's the reusable skeleton: the depth illusion comes purely from per-layer scale + speed + brightness, never from the field you happen to plug in.

    Brief SwiftUI usage

    Drive time from a TimelineView so the layers drift:

    TimelineView(.animation) { ctx in
    let t = ctx.date.timeIntervalSinceReferenceDate
    Color.black
        .visualEffect { content, proxy in
            content.colorEffect(
                ShaderLibrary.layeredField(.float2(proxy.size), .float(t))
            )
        }
}

    Shadertoy names map straight across: iTime is time, iResolution is size.

    A word on performance

    Every layer is a full evaluation of the field. Fifteen fbm layers means fifteen fbm calls per pixel, per frame — the cost scales linearly with LAYERS and with how expensive one field sample is. A cheap hash spark is nearly free; an eight-octave fbm times fifteen is not. Tune LAYERS down, or use cheaper fields for the far, dim layers where detail is wasted anyway.

    The foundation

    • motion parallax = the brain reads difference in speed as depth; near things move fast, far things move slow.
    • layering = stack many copies of one field and add them, giving volume without real 3D.
    • fi depth ramp = a single 0→1 value per layer that drives scale, speed, and brightness via mix.
    • scale/speed/brightness gradient = far layers are smaller, slower, and dimmer — this trio is the depth illusion.
    • decorrelating offset = a per-layer spatial shove (fi * 13.7) so layers don't sample in lockstep.
    • additive accumulation = summing layers stacks brightness like real light and haze, building apparent density.
    • field is swappable = replace the placeholder spark with Voronoi, glows, or fbm; the layering skeleton stays.
    • linear cost = each layer is a full field evaluation, so cost grows with LAYERS times field expense.

    Related

    29-sdf-toolkit · 26-advanced-noise-fbm · 23-animation-in-shaders

    Prev: 29-sdf-toolkit · Next: 31-stateless-procedural-animation · Hub: 00-roadmap

    Prev← 29 — The SDF Toolkit: Shapes, Smooth-Min & Soft ParticlesNext31 — Stateless Procedural Animation: Motion Without Memory →

    31 — Stateless Procedural Animation: Motion Without Memory

    Start here — the plain idea

    A fragment shader has amnesia. It runs fresh for every pixel, every frame, and when it's done it remembers nothing — no positions saved, no velocities carried over, no "where was this ember last frame?" The next frame starts from a blank slate. And yet on screen, smoke curls upward, sparks orbit, embers rise and wink out. How?

    The trick is that motion isn't stored, it's computed. Everything you see is a pure function of two inputs: the pixel's coordinate uv and the clock time. Change time a little and the same formula produces a slightly-shifted picture; play those pictures in sequence and it looks like continuous motion. Because the formula is deterministic, frame 100 and frame 101 line up perfectly — there's no drift or jitter, even though nothing was passed between them.

    Think of a flip-book where each page isn't drawn by hand but calculated from its page number. Page 42 doesn't need to peek at page 41; it just plugs 42 into the formula and out comes the right frame. That's the opposite of a CPU particle system, which keeps arrays of positions and velocities and nudges them each tick. The shader keeps nothing — and as 18b-gestures-to-shaders explains, it can't: there's no way to write state back. So we lean into it. Motion here is (uv, time) → look, recomputed from scratch, forever.

    Pattern 1: scroll a whole field by time

    The simplest animation is to slide the coordinate you sample at. Move where you look, and the whole pattern appears to flow.

    float f = fbm(uv * 3.0 + float2(0.0, time * 0.2));   // smoke drifting up

    fbm(uv * 3.0) is a static cloud of noise (fractal Brownian motion — see 15-noise-and-patterns and 26-advanced-noise-fbm). Adding float2(0.0, time * 0.2) shifts the sampling point along Y as time advances. Since we're reading the noise field at a coordinate that keeps sliding, the pattern appears to drift upward at speed 0.2 — smoke rising. Nothing about the noise is stored or regenerated; we simply look at a different part of it each frame. Swap the offset to float2(time * 0.2, 0.0) for horizontal flow, or feed noise into the offset itself for a swirl.

    Pattern 2: per-element independent motion via a hashed phase

    Scrolling moves everything together. To make individual particles or cells move independently — each with its own rhythm — give each one a private random seed, and derive its motion from that seed.

    float2 hash22(float2 p){ p = float2(dot(p,float2(127.1,311.7)), dot(p,float2(269.5,183.3))); return fract(sin(p)*43758.5453); }
float2 cellPoint(float2 cell, float time){
    float2 seed = hash22(cell);
    return 0.5 + 0.4 * sin(time * (0.5 + seed) + 6.2831 * seed);   // each cell orbits at its own rate/phase
}

    hash22 turns a 2D input into a repeatable 2D pseudo-random value in [0,1). It's deterministic: the same cell always hashes to the same seed, which is exactly why we can rebuild a particle's motion every frame without storing it — the seed is recomputed, not remembered.

    cellPoint uses that seed twice inside a sin. time * (0.5 + seed) sets each cell's rate — a cell with a bigger seed oscillates faster — so no two cells share a tempo. + 6.2831 * seed (that's 2π · seed) sets each cell's phase, a random starting point in its cycle, so they're not all at the peak of their swing at once. The 0.5 + 0.4 * sin(...) wraps it into a gentle orbit centered in the cell. The result: a grid of points each drifting on its own little loop, all from a hash and the clock. This is the heart of animated Voronoi, twinkling stars, and jittering particle fields.

    Pattern 3: loop a lifetime without storing it

    Particles are born, rise, fade, and die — a cycle. You'd think you need to track each particle's age. You don't: fract gives you a repeating 0→1 ramp for free.

    float life = fract(time * 0.3 + hash22(cell).x);   // 0→1 repeating; use for rise + fade

    time * 0.3 is an ever-climbing number; fract keeps only its fractional part, so it sweeps 0 → 1 then snaps back to 0 and climbs again — a sawtooth that loops every 1/0.3 ≈ 3.3 seconds. Adding hash22(cell).x offsets each cell's phase so they don't all reset in unison. Read life as normalized age: 0 is just-born, 1 is about-to-die. You map it to whatever a particle's life should do — position (rise as life grows), opacity (fade as life → 1), size, color. When it wraps to 0 the "next" particle begins, no bookkeeping required.

    Putting it together: rising, fading embers

    Here's a small stitchable shader combining pattern 1's coordinate-scroll idea with pattern 3's looping life — a field of embers that rise and fade.

    float2 hash22(float2 p){ p = float2(dot(p,float2(127.1,311.7)), dot(p,float2(269.5,183.3))); return fract(sin(p)*43758.5453); }

[[ stitchable ]] half4 embers(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size, float time) {
    float2 uv = (position - 0.5*size) / size.y;
    half3 col = half3(0.0h);
    for (int i = 0; i < 12; i++) {
        float2 cell = float2(float(i), 0.0);
        float2 h = hash22(cell);
        float life = fract(time * 0.3 + h.x);          // normalized age, looping
        float x = (h.y - 0.5) * 1.2 + 0.02 * sin(time + h.x * 6.2831); // lane + gentle sway
        float y = mix(-0.5, 0.6, life);                // rise from bottom to top
        float fade = life * (1.0 - life) * 4.0;        // 0 at birth/death, 1 mid-life
        float d = length(uv - float2(x, y));
        col += half3(1.0h, 0.5h, 0.15h) * half(fade * exp(-d * 40.0)); // warm glow
    }
    return half4(col, 1.0h);
}

    Each of the 12 embers gets a stable hash22 seed from its index. life is its looping age; y = mix(-0.5, 0.6, life) carries it from below the screen up past the top as it ages. x places it in a horizontal lane (from h.y) with a small time-driven sway so it doesn't rise ramrod-straight. fade = life*(1-life)*4 is a parabola that's 0 at birth and death and peaks at mid-life — so each ember brightens in, then dims out, exactly once per loop. exp(-d * 40.0) is the soft glow falloff from 29-sdf-toolkit, tinted warm and added in. Every ember is reconstructed from its index and time alone — recompute the frame at any moment and you get a consistent picture.

    Brief SwiftUI usage

    Feed time from a TimelineView and the whole thing animates:

    TimelineView(.animation) { ctx in
    let t = ctx.date.timeIntervalSinceReferenceDate
    Color.black
        .visualEffect { content, proxy in
            content.colorEffect(
                ShaderLibrary.embers(.float2(proxy.size), .float(t))
            )
        }
}

    As always, Shadertoy's iTime is our time and iResolution is size (more on the animation setup in 23-animation-in-shaders).

    Why this matters

    The payoff of statelessness is scale. Because every pixel's value depends only on (uv, time) and nothing else, there are no buffers to allocate, no arrays to update, no data dependency between pixels. The GPU can compute millions of pixels fully in parallel, each in isolation, and it all stays perfectly in sync because they share the same deterministic formula and the same clock. A CPU particle system fights memory and update order; a procedural shader sidesteps both by refusing to remember anything at all.

    The foundation

    • stateless = a fragment shader stores nothing between frames; each frame is recomputed from scratch.
    • motion = f(uv, time) = animation is a pure, deterministic function of coordinate and clock, not saved state.
    • coordinate scroll = adding time to the sampling coordinate slides a whole field (drifting smoke).
    • hashed seed = hash22(cell) gives each element a repeatable random value, so its motion can be rebuilt every frame.
    • per-element phase & rate = feeding the seed into sin(time*rate + phase) makes cells move independently.
    • fract lifetime = fract(time*speed + seed) is a looping 0→1 age for rise/fade with no stored counter.
    • determinism = same (uv, time) always yields the same look, so frames line up and motion stays continuous.
    • free scaling = no buffers and no inter-pixel dependency means the GPU runs millions of pixels in parallel.

    Related

    18b-gestures-to-shaders · 23-animation-in-shaders · 30-layering-and-parallax · 26-advanced-noise-fbm

    Prev: 30-layering-and-parallax · Next: 32-cosine-palettes · Hub: 00-roadmap

    Prev← 30 — Layering & Fake Parallax: Volumetric Depth Without 3DNext32 — Cosine Palettes: Infinite Color From Four Numbers →

    32 — Cosine Palettes: Infinite Color From Four Numbers

    Start here — the plain idea

    Imagine three little dials, one each for Red, Green, and Blue. Each dial slowly sweeps up and down as you turn a single master knob t. Because they sweep at slightly different rates and start at slightly different points, they never all peak at once — so as t slides from 0 to 1 you get a smooth, ever-shifting parade of color: warm ambers, teals, violets, back to warm. That is a cosine palette. One number in, a beautiful color out.

    The magic is that a cosine is just a smooth wave that rolls between two values forever. If you build R, G, and B each from their own cosine wave, and give each channel its own starting phase, you get a gradient that loops seamlessly and looks hand-tuned — without storing a single texture or gradient stop.

    Inigo Quilez boiled the whole thing down to four float3 constants: a, b, c, d. Feed any scalar you already have lying around — fbm, distance from center, an angle, time — and this is how nearly every gorgeous Shadertoy gradient gets its color. It is cheap (GPUs love cos), resolution-independent, and endlessly tweakable.

    The palette function

    half3 palette(float t, float3 a, float3 b, float3 c, float3 d){
    return half3(a + b * cos(6.28318 * (c * t + d)));    // 6.28318 = 2π
}
// A classic warm-to-cool rainbow:
//   a = float3(0.5), b = float3(0.5), c = float3(1.0), d = float3(0.00, 0.33, 0.67)

    Read it as one cosine wave per channel, evaluated all three at once because everything is a float3:

    • t is your input scalar. It can be anything in roughly 0..1 — the master knob. When t grows past 1 the palette simply loops, which is often exactly what you want.
    • c * t + d is the phase fed into the cosine. c scales t (how fast we spin), d offsets it (where we start).
    • 6.28318 is 2π. Multiplying by it means "one full color cycle happens when c * t advances by 1," so the numbers stay human-friendly.
    • cos(...) returns a value in -1..1 for each channel.
    • b * cos(...) scales that wave to -b..b. b is the amplitude.
    • a + ... shifts the whole wave up so it oscillates around a. So each channel ranges over a - b .. a + b.

    Now the four dials in plain words:

    • a — base color. The midpoint each channel wobbles around. float3(0.5) centers everything at mid-grey so colors stay balanced.
    • b — contrast / amplitude. How far each channel swings from a. Bigger b = punchier, more saturated. Small b = washed-out, pastel. With a = b = 0.5 the channels sweep the full 0..1 range.
    • c — frequency (cycles per channel). How many times each channel goes through the rainbow as t runs 0→1. c = 1 gives one clean sweep; c = 3 packs three color bands into the same range.
    • d — phase offset per channel. Where each channel starts in its cycle. The classic (0.00, 0.33, 0.67) staggers R, G, B by a third of a cycle each, which is precisely what makes it read as a rainbow instead of grey.

    Driving it in a stitchable shader

    Here we color an fbm field — but notice the last line is all that changes if you swap in radial distance or time.

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 cosPalette(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size, float time){
    float2 uv = position / size;

    // pick ONE scalar to drive the color:
    float t = fbm(uv * 3.0 + time * 0.1);        // noisy, organic
    // float t = length(uv - 0.5) * 2.0;          // radial rings
    // float t = fract(time * 0.1);               // pure time sweep

    float3 a = float3(0.5);
    float3 b = float3(0.5);
    float3 c = float3(1.0);
    float3 d = float3(0.00, 0.33, 0.67);

    half3 col = palette(t, a, b, c, d);
    return half4(col, 1.0h);
}

    fbm is the fractal noise from 26-advanced-noise-fbm; uv = position / size maps pixels into 0..1 as usual. Shadertoy readers: time is iTime, size is iResolution. Animate it from SwiftUI so time advances:

    TimelineView(.animation) { ctx in
    let t = ctx.date.timeIntervalSinceReferenceDate
    Rectangle()
        .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.cosPalette(.float(t)))
}

    Presets — and what each dial does to them

    // Fire: warm, low green, no blue — d holds R ahead, kills the cool end
//   a = float3(0.5, 0.3, 0.2)
//   b = float3(0.5, 0.3, 0.2)
//   c = float3(1.0, 1.0, 1.0)
//   d = float3(0.0, 0.1, 0.2)

// Ocean: blue-green dominant, quiet red
//   a = float3(0.2, 0.5, 0.6)
//   b = float3(0.2, 0.4, 0.5)
//   c = float3(1.0, 1.0, 1.0)
//   d = float3(0.5, 0.4, 0.3)

// Nebula: high frequency + offset phases = magenta/cyan banding
//   a = float3(0.5, 0.5, 0.5)
//   b = float3(0.5, 0.5, 0.5)
//   c = float3(2.0, 1.0, 1.0)
//   d = float3(0.5, 0.2, 0.25)

    Three knobs to feel out by hand:

    • Shift the hue: move d. Adding the same amount to all of d rotates the whole palette through the rainbow — a free "hue slider." Nudge one channel of d and you recolor just that channel's timing.
    • Add bands: raise c. Going from c = 1 to c = 2 or 3 makes each channel cycle more times across the same t, so a smooth gradient turns into stripes or shells. Great for contour lines and nebula filaments.
    • Flatten or pop contrast: change b. Shrink b toward 0 and the color collapses toward the flat base a (pastel, muted). Grow b and the swings get bold and saturated. Keep a + b ≤ 1 and a - b ≥ 0 to avoid clipping.

    Because it is just three cosines, it costs almost nothing per pixel and looks identical at any resolution. Pair it with 28-domain-warping to color a warped field — feed the warped coordinate's length or an fbm sample straight into t — and you get those liquid, marbled Shadertoy looks for free.

    Credit and a live editor for the four constants: Inigo Quilez, "Palettes" (iquilezles.org/articles/palettes).

    The foundation

    • cosine palette = smooth procedural color from a + b·cos(2π(c·t + d)), one wave per RGB channel.
    • a (base) = the midpoint each channel oscillates around; sets overall brightness/tint.
    • b (amplitude) = swing size; controls contrast and saturation, flat when small.
    • c (frequency) = color cycles across t; raise it to add bands and rings.
    • d (phase) = per-channel start offset; the staggered (0, 0.33, 0.67) is what makes a rainbow, and shifting it slides the hue.
    • t (input) = any scalar you have — fbm, distance, angle, or time — mapped to a color.
    • cheap & scalable = a few cos per pixel, no textures, identical at every resolution.

    Related

    28-domain-warping · 26-advanced-noise-fbm · 04-coordinates-and-color

    Prev: 31-stateless-procedural-animation · Next: 33-post-processing · Hub: 00-roadmap

    Prev← 31 — Stateless Procedural Animation: Motion Without MemoryNext33 — Post-Processing: Bloom, Vignette, Grain & Grade →

    33 — Post-Processing: Bloom, Vignette, Grain & Grade

    Start here — the plain idea

    You have made a shape. Maybe a glowing SDF, maybe a noisy nebula. It is fine — but it looks rendered, a little clinical. Post-processing is the pass you run after the main image to turn "a shape" into "a mood." It is the difference between a raw photo and one that has been through a film lab.

    Think of these as the Instagram filters of shaders: bloom makes bright things bleed light, a vignette gently darkens the corners so your eye lands in the middle, grain adds a faint living texture, chromatic aberration fakes the color fringing of a real lens, and tone mapping tames blown-out highlights so nothing clips to ugly flat white. None of them are big; together they read as "produced."

    One important split in SwiftUI. Effects that only need this pixel — vignette, grain, tone mapping — are colorEffect shaders. Effects that need to peek at neighboring pixels — bloom (blur the bright parts), chromatic aberration (sample the image at an offset) — are layerEffect shaders, because those get a sampler over the whole rendered layer. See 18-metal-in-swiftui for the layerEffect mechanics and 13-blur-effects for the full neighbor-sampling blur.

    The per-pixel toolkit

    // vignette (per-pixel)
float vig(float2 uv){ float2 q = uv*(1.0-uv); return pow(q.x*q.y*15.0, 0.25); }   // ~1 center → 0 edges

// film grain
float grain(float2 uv, float time){ return fract(sin(dot(uv, float2(12.99,78.23)) + time)*43758.55); }

// Reinhard tone map
half3 tonemap(half3 c){ return c / (c + 1.0h); }

// ACES-ish (punchier)
half3 aces(half3 x){ return clamp((x*(2.51h*x+0.03h))/(x*(2.43h*x+0.59h)+0.14h), 0.0h, 1.0h); }

    vig(uv) — the parabola q = uv*(1-uv) peaks at 0.25 in the center of each axis and falls to 0 at both edges (0 and 1). Multiplying q.x*q.y gives a 2D bump that is brightest dead-center. The *15.0 rescales that small product back up toward 1 in the middle, and pow(..., 0.25) softens the falloff so the darkening rolls off gently instead of a hard oval. Result: ~1 in the center, →0 at the corners. Multiply your color by it.

    grain(uv, time) — the classic hash: take a dot product of the pixel coordinate with two "magic" irrational-ish numbers, add time so the pattern reshuffles every frame, feed it through sin and *43758.55, then fract to keep only the chaotic fractional part. Out comes pseudo-random noise in 0..1, different per pixel and per frame — TV static you can dial down to a whisper.

    tonemap(c) (Reinhard) — c / (c + 1) maps 0..∞ into 0..1: dark values pass almost unchanged, but very bright values get gently compressed instead of clipping to flat white. This is how you keep HDR-ish bloom and lights from turning into featureless blobs.

    aces(x) — a cheap approximation of the film-industry ACES curve. Same job as Reinhard but with more contrast and richer highlights — punchier, more "cinematic." Use whichever you like the look of; do not use both.

    Neighbor-sampling effects (layerEffect)

    These need the whole rendered image as a sampler, so in SwiftUI they are layerEffect shaders with a SwiftUI::Layer argument. Keep them short here — 13-blur-effects has the real multi-tap blur.

    #include <SwiftUI/SwiftUI.h>

// Chromatic aberration: sample R shifted one way, B the other, keep G centered.
[[ stitchable ]] half4 aberrate(float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer, float2 size, float amount){
    float2 dir = (position - 0.5*size) / size.y;     // outward from center
    float2 off = dir * amount;
    half r = layer.sample(position + off).r;         // red pushed out
    half g = layer.sample(position).g;               // green stays put
    half b = layer.sample(position - off).b;         // blue pulled in
    return half4(r, g, b, 1.0h);
}

// Cheap "bloom": average a few taps, keep only the bright part, add it back.
[[ stitchable ]] half4 bloom(float2 position, SwiftUI::Layer layer, float2 size, float time){
    half4 src = layer.sample(position);
    half4 blur = 0.0h;
    for (int i = -2; i <= 2; i++)
        for (int j = -2; j <= 2; j++)
            blur += layer.sample(position + float2(i, j) * 2.0);
    blur /= 25.0h;
    half3 bright = max(blur.rgb - 0.6h, 0.0h);        // threshold: only glowing parts
    return half4(src.rgb + bright, 1.0h);             // add the glow on top
}

    The idea, not the optimization: aberration samples each color channel at a slightly different position so the image's colors separate at the edges, exactly like a cheap lens; the offset grows with distance from center so the middle stays sharp. Bloom blurs the layer (a tiny 5×5 box average here — swap in the proper Gaussian from 13-blur-effects), subtracts a threshold so only the bright regions survive, then adds that soft bright ghost back onto the original. Bright things now bleed light.

    Composing the pass

    Order matters. Tone map first (bring HDR into range), then darken the edges, then sprinkle grain on top so the noise is not itself compressed:

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 grade(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size, float time){
    float2 uv = position / size;
    half3 col = color.rgb;

    col = tonemap(col);                 // squash highlights into 0..1
    col *= vig(uv);                     // darken the corners
    col += grain(uv, time) * 0.04;      // faint living texture (0.04 = subtle)

    return half4(col, color.a);
}

    That is a full color grade in three lines. Keep grain low (0.02–0.06); more than that reads as "broken," not "film." In SwiftUI wire the animated ones with a clock:

    TimelineView(.animation) { ctx in
    let t = ctx.date.timeIntervalSinceReferenceDate
    myView
        .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.grade(.float(t)))                 // per-pixel grade
        .layerEffect(ShaderLibrary.bloom(.float(t)), maxSampleOffset: .init(width: 4, height: 4))
}

    Note the maxSampleOffset on the layerEffect — it tells SwiftUI how far your neighbor taps reach.

    One more polish: cheap anti-aliasing

    When you draw shapes from an SDF, a hard step(0, d) edge shimmers and jaggies. Fade across exactly one pixel instead, using fwidth (how fast d changes between neighboring pixels):

    float fw = fwidth(d);
float mask = smoothstep(fw, -fw, d);   // 1 inside the shape, 0 outside, soft over ~1px

    Because fw scales with resolution automatically, the edge stays one pixel wide whether the view is tiny or Retina. SDF details live in 29-sdf-toolkit and 04-coordinates-and-color.

    The foundation

    • post-processing = the polish pass run after the main image; turns a shape into a mood.
    • colorEffect vs layerEffect = per-pixel effects (vignette, grain, tonemap) vs neighbor-sampling ones (bloom, aberration) that need a SwiftUI::Layer.
    • vignette = darken toward the edges so the eye lands center; multiply color by vig(uv).
    • film grain = per-pixel, per-frame hash noise added faintly (~0.04) for texture.
    • tone mapping = compress HDR into 0..1 without clipping; Reinhard is soft, ACES is punchier.
    • bloom = blur the bright parts past a threshold and add them back for glow.
    • chromatic aberration = sample R and B at opposite offsets for lens-style color fringing.
    • fwidth AA = smoothstep(fw, -fw, d) fades an SDF edge over one resolution-independent pixel.

    Related

    13-blur-effects · 18-metal-in-swiftui · 29-sdf-toolkit · 26-advanced-noise-fbm

    Prev: 32-cosine-palettes · Next: 34-raymarching-3d-sdf · Hub: 00-roadmap

    Prev← 32 — Cosine Palettes: Infinite Color From Four NumbersNext34 — Raymarching & 3D SDFs: Shadertoy-Style 3D →

    34 — Raymarching & 3D SDFs: Shadertoy-Style 3D

    Start here — the plain idea

    Every 3D scene you have seen on Shadertoy — glossy spheres, infinite floors, gooey blobs, whole alien landscapes — was drawn by a flat 2D fragment shader with no meshes at all. No vertices, no triangles, no geometry buffer. Just math that answers one question for any point in space: "how far am I from the nearest surface?" That function is a 3D signed distance field, map(p).

    Here is the trick that makes it work. Picture walking toward a wall through thick fog, blindfolded, but you hold a magic compass that always tells you the exact distance to the nearest object in any direction. If it says "3 meters," you can safely stride 3 meters forward — nothing can be closer than that, so you cannot bump into anything. Take another reading, stride again. Each step you get closer, the safe distance shrinks, your strides get smaller, and you converge right onto the surface. That is sphere tracing: step forward by exactly the SDF's value, repeat until the distance is tiny (you have hit) or you have gone too far (you missed).

    Once a ray hits, you find the surface's normal by sampling how the distance field changes in each direction — its gradient — and then do ordinary lighting with it. This is heavier than everything before: it is a whole loop per pixel, so we cap the step count and the max distance. But it is the finale technique — the door to real procedural 3D. Bring your 2D SDF intuition from 29-sdf-toolkit and your lighting from 22-lighting-and-normals.

    The whole thing

    float sdSphere(float3 p, float r){ return length(p) - r; }

float map(float3 p){                          // the whole scene as one distance
    float s = sdSphere(p, 1.0);
    float ground = p.y + 1.0;                  // a floor plane
    return min(s, ground);
}

float3 calcNormal(float3 p){                   // gradient of the field = surface normal
    float2 e = float2(0.001, 0.0);
    return normalize(float3(
        map(p+e.xyy) - map(p-e.xyy),
        map(p+e.yxy) - map(p-e.yxy),
        map(p+e.yyx) - map(p-e.yyx)));
}

[[ stitchable ]] half4 raymarch(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size, float time){
    float2 uv = (position - 0.5*size) / size.y;
    float3 ro = float3(0.0, 0.0, 3.0);         // ray origin (camera)
    float3 rd = normalize(float3(uv, -1.5));   // ray direction
    float t = 0.0;
    for (int i = 0; i < 80; i++){              // sphere trace
        float3 p = ro + rd * t;
        float d = map(p);
        if (d < 0.001 || t > 20.0) break;      // hit, or gone too far
        t += d;                                // safe step = distance to nearest surface
    }
    half3 col = half3(0.02h);                  // background
    if (t < 20.0){
        float3 p = ro + rd * t;
        float3 n = calcNormal(p);
        float3 lightDir = normalize(float3(0.8, 0.7, 0.5));
        float diff = max(dot(n, lightDir), 0.0);
        col = half3(0.2h) + half3(diff);       // ambient + diffuse
    }
    return half4(col, 1.0h);
}

    The scene: map(p)

    sdSphere is the simplest 3D SDF: the distance from point p to a sphere of radius r is just length(p) - r — positive outside, zero on the shell, negative inside. map(p) is your entire world expressed as distance. Here it holds two things: the sphere, and a ground plane. The plane's distance is beautifully simple — p.y + 1.0 is how far a point is above the horizontal plane at y = -1. Then min(s, ground) unions them: the distance to the nearest of the two surfaces. Union of SDFs is just min. Everything you place in the scene funnels through this one min/max/smin expression.

    The march loop

    uv = (position - 0.5*size) / size.y centers coordinates on the screen and divides by height so the aspect ratio is correct (this is the centered/aspect form from the coordinates lesson).

    • ro — the ray origin, i.e. the camera, sitting at z = 3 looking toward the origin.
    • rd — the ray direction for this pixel: aim through the pixel's uv with z = -1.5 (into the screen), normalized to unit length. The -1.5 is effectively the focal length; smaller magnitude = wider field of view.
    • The loop is the sphere trace. Each iteration: p = ro + rd * t is the current point along the ray; d = map(p) is the safe distance to the nearest surface; if d is tiny we have hit, and if t has run past 20 we have missed empty space — either way, break. Otherwise t += d strides forward by exactly that safe distance. That single line is the whole magic: because the SDF guarantees nothing is closer than d, stepping d can never overshoot a surface.
    • 80 is the step cap and 20.0 the max distance — the performance guardrails. Too few steps and grazing rays give up before converging (you see fuzzy silhouettes); too many and it is slow. This is the cost of raymarching: a bounded loop for every pixel.

    The normal from the gradient

    calcNormal asks: which way does the distance field increase fastest? That direction is the outward surface normal. We estimate it by finite differences — sample map a hair (0.001) in the + and - direction along each axis and subtract. The e.xyy, e.yxy, e.yyx swizzles are a compact way to build the offset vectors (0.001,0,0), (0,0.001,0), (0,0,0.001) since e = (0.001, 0). Six map calls, normalize, done — no stored normals anywhere.

    The shading

    With a normal in hand it is textbook diffuse lighting (see 22-lighting-and-normals): diff = max(dot(n, lightDir), 0.0) — surfaces facing the light are bright, surfaces facing away clamp to 0. col = ambient + diff adds a flat 0.2 fill so shadowed sides are not pure black. Rays that missed keep the dark 0.02 background.

    Animating and combining

    Because the scene is just code, animation is trivial — perturb map with time. Bob the sphere up and down:

    float map(float3 p, float time){
    float3 c = float3(0.0, sin(time) * 0.5, 0.0);   // moving center
    float s = sdSphere(p - c, 1.0);
    float ground = p.y + 1.0;
    return min(s, ground);
}

    Subtracting a center c from p translates the sphere — a general SDF move: to place a shape at c, evaluate the shape at p - c. Drive time from SwiftUI with the animation clock:

    TimelineView(.animation) { ctx in
    let t = ctx.date.timeIntervalSinceReferenceDate
    Rectangle()
        .colorEffect(ShaderLibrary.raymarch(.float(t)))
}

    To orbit the camera, rotate ro (and re-aim rd at the origin) with a 2D rotation on its x/z using sin(time)/cos(time). To make shapes melt together, swap the min in map for the smooth minimum smin from 29-sdf-toolkit — instead of a hard crease where two SDFs meet you get a gooey, liquid blend, the signature look of metaballs in 3D. Add more primitives (boxes, tori), union or subtract them, and you are building whole scenes out of nothing but distance.

    Shadertoy note: time is iTime, size is iResolution. Most 3D shaders you find online are exactly this skeleton — a richer map, fancier lighting, and shadows/AO layered on top.

    The foundation

    • 3D SDF = a function map(p) giving signed distance from any point to the nearest surface; negative inside.
    • sphere tracing = step along each ray by exactly map(p); the safe distance guarantees no overshoot.
    • ray origin / direction = ro is the camera, rd is the per-pixel look direction into the scene.
    • step & distance caps = the for limit and t > max bail-out; raymarching's per-pixel loop cost lives here.
    • union / blend = min joins SDFs into one scene; smin blends them gooily.
    • gradient normal = surface normal from finite-difference samples of map around the hit point.
    • shading = max(dot(n, lightDir), 0) diffuse plus an ambient fill, using the SDF-derived normal.
    • translate a shape = evaluate it at p - center; animate by driving center (or the camera) with time.

    Related

    29-sdf-toolkit · 22-lighting-and-normals · 04-coordinates-and-color · 26-advanced-noise-fbm

    Prev: 33-post-processing · Next: 35-capstone-smoke-sparks-nebula · Hub: 00-roadmap

    Prev← 33 — Post-Processing: Bloom, Vignette, Grain & GradeNext35 — Capstone: A Drifting Smoke / Sparks / Nebula →

    35 — Capstone: A Drifting Smoke / Sparks / Nebula

    Start here — the plain idea

    This is where the whole advanced cluster pays off. On its own, each technique is small — but stacked, they make the kind of shader that looks like it took a studio to build. We're going to assemble a living nebula: soft drifting smoke, glowing embers, colored by a palette, with fake depth — and not a single particle stored anywhere. Every frame is recomputed from (uv, time).

    The recipe is just the last nine lessons, layered like a cake:

    1. fBm (26-advanced-noise-fbm) makes the base cloud shape.
    2. Domain warping (28-domain-warping) makes it flow instead of sitting still.
    3. Voronoi (27-voronoi-deep-dive) sprinkles in glowing sparks.
    4. Layering (30-layering-and-parallax) stacks a few offset copies for depth.
    5. Cosine palette (32-cosine-palettes) colors the density.
    6. Stateless animation (31-stateless-procedural-animation) drives all of it from time.
    7. Post (33-post-processing) — a vignette and a whisper of grain to finish.

    Read it as "density → color → depth → polish." Once you see how the pieces snap together, you can rebuild any of them into your own effect.

    The building blocks (from the earlier lessons)

    Paste the helpers you already met — hash, value noise, fBm, and the Voronoi and palette functions. They're unchanged; this is just gathering them in one file.

    #include <metal_stdlib>
#include <SwiftUI/SwiftUI.h>
using namespace metal;

float hash21(float2 p){ p = fract(p*float2(123.34, 345.45)); p += dot(p, p+34.345); return fract(p.x*p.y); }

float valueNoise(float2 p){
    float2 i = floor(p), f = fract(p);
    float a = hash21(i), b = hash21(i+float2(1,0)), c = hash21(i+float2(0,1)), d = hash21(i+float2(1,1));
    float2 u = f*f*(3.0-2.0*f);
    return mix(mix(a,b,u.x), mix(c,d,u.x), u.y);
}

float fbm(float2 p){
    float sum = 0.0, amp = 0.5, freq = 1.0;
    for (int i = 0; i < 6; i++){ sum += amp*valueNoise(p*freq); freq *= 2.0; amp *= 0.5; }
    return sum;
}

float2 hash22(float2 p){
    p = float2(dot(p, float2(127.1,311.7)), dot(p, float2(269.5,183.3)));
    return fract(sin(p)*43758.5453);
}

// Voronoi F1 (nearest feature-point distance) — for sparks
float voronoiF1(float2 x, float time){
    float2 n = floor(x), f = fract(x);
    float f1 = 8.0;
    for (int j=-1;j<=1;j++)
    for (int i=-1;i<=1;i++){
        float2 g = float2(i,j);
        float2 o = hash22(n+g);
        o = 0.5 + 0.5*sin(time*0.6 + 6.2831*o);   // sparks twinkle in place
        float2 r = g + o - f;
        f1 = min(f1, dot(r,r));
    }
    return sqrt(f1);
}

// Inigo Quilez cosine palette — nebula colors
half3 palette(float t){
    float3 a = float3(0.35, 0.20, 0.45);   // base (violet)
    float3 b = float3(0.45, 0.35, 0.55);   // amplitude
    float3 c = float3(1.0,  1.0,  1.0);    // frequency
    float3 d = float3(0.10, 0.25, 0.55);   // phase → magenta→blue sweep
    return half3(a + b * cos(6.28318 * (c*t + d)));
}

    The nebula shader

    Now the main event. Read the comments top to bottom — each block is one lesson doing its job.

    [[ stitchable ]] half4 nebula(float2 position, half4 color, float2 size, float time) {
    // centered, aspect-correct coordinates (see lesson 04 / 29)
    float2 uv = (position - 0.5*size) / size.y;

    half3 col = half3(0.0h);

    // --- LAYERING: a few offset copies for volumetric depth (lesson 30) ---
    const int LAYERS = 5;
    for (int i = 0; i < LAYERS; i++) {
        float fi    = float(i) / float(LAYERS);
        float scale = mix(1.2, 3.0, fi);            // far layers finer
        float speed = mix(0.10, 0.03, fi);          // far layers slower
        float dim   = mix(1.0, 0.35, fi);           // far layers dimmer

        float2 p = uv * scale + float2(fi*7.3, time*speed);

        // --- DOMAIN WARP: bend the coordinates by noise so smoke flows (lesson 28) ---
        float2 q = float2(fbm(p + float2(0.0, time*0.05)),
                          fbm(p + float2(5.2, 1.3)));
        float density = fbm(p + 3.0*q);             // warped fBm = drifting smoke (lessons 26, 28)

        // --- COLOR: palette driven by the local density (lesson 32) ---
        half3 smoke = palette(density + fi*0.15) * half(density * density);

        // --- SPARKS: Voronoi points glowing through the smoke (lesson 27) ---
        float f1    = voronoiF1(p*1.5, time);
        float spark = smoothstep(0.10, 0.0, f1);    // tight bright dot per cell
        half3 ember = half3(1.0h, 0.75h, 0.4h) * half(spark) * half(density);

        col += (smoke + ember) * half(dim);          // additive accumulation
    }

    // --- POST: tone map + vignette + a little grain (lesson 33) ---
    col = col / (col + 0.7h);                                    // Reinhard-ish tone map
    float2 vq = (position/size) * (1.0 - position/size);
    col *= half(pow(vq.x*vq.y*15.0, 0.25));                       // vignette
    col += half(fract(sin(dot(position, float2(12.99,78.23)) + time)*43758.55) * 0.03 - 0.015); // grain

    return half4(col, 1.0h);
}

    Everything you see moving is time scrolling the sampling coordinates — there is no particle array, no simulation state. Each pixel independently recomputes its color from (uv, time), which is exactly why this runs at full frame rate on millions of pixels.

    Hook it up in SwiftUI

    struct NebulaView: View {
    var body: some View {
        TimelineView(.animation) { tl in
            let t = Float(tl.date.timeIntervalSinceReferenceDate)
            Rectangle()
                .visualEffect { content, proxy in
                    content.colorEffect(
                        ShaderLibrary.nebula(.float2(proxy.size), .float(t))
                    )
                }
                .ignoresSafeArea()
        }
    }
}

    That's it — a full animated nebula behind your UI in ~40 lines of shader.

    Make it yours (the point of the exercise)

    Every knob here maps to a lesson, so tuning it is the review:

    • More/thicker smoke → raise the fBm octaves or the 3.0*q warp strength (26, 28).
    • Wilder flow → add a second warp level r before the final fbm (28).
    • Denser sparks → raise the Voronoi frequency p*1.5, or widen the smoothstep(0.10, 0.0, f1) (27).
    • Different mood → swap the four palette constants — try a fire preset or an ocean one (32).
    • More depth → raise LAYERS, spread the scale/speed/dim ranges wider (30).
    • Calmer or faster → scale time up or down where it enters each block (31).

    Build it once, then spend ten minutes turning knobs. That muscle — "I want this feeling, which knob is it?" — is the whole skill, and it's the same Choosing the Math instinct from the interaction course, now in pixels.

    The foundation

    • the recipe = density (warped fBm) → color (palette) → sparks (Voronoi) → depth (layering) → polish (post) — assembled, not invented.
    • domain-warped fBm = the flowing smoke base; the warp is what makes it drift instead of freeze.
    • Voronoi F1 = one glowing spark per cell, twinkling via a time-animated point.
    • cosine palette = turns a scalar density into nebula color from four constants.
    • layering = a few offset copies with different scale/speed/brightness fake volumetric depth.
    • stateless = the entire scene is a pure function of (uv, time) — no particles stored, so it's cheap and infinitely scalable.
    • post = tone map + vignette + grain is the 3-line difference between "a shape" and "a mood."
    • the real skill = knowing which knob produces which feeling — pure Choosing the Math, in shader form.

    Related

    • The techniques it combines: 26-advanced-noise-fbm, 27-voronoi-deep-dive, 28-domain-warping, 30-layering-and-parallax, 32-cosine-palettes, 33-post-processing
    • Driving it from SwiftUI: 18a-feeding-data-into-shaders, 18-metal-in-swiftui
    • The 3D sibling technique: 34-raymarching-3d-sdf

    Card Shader Reflections — PBR Metallic Card

    Start here — the plain idea

    Picture one of those shiny metal credit cards — silver, gold, platinum — and how the light slides across the surface as you tilt it. That's the effect here: a flat rectangle that looks like polished metal, with reflections that move as you scroll.

    Nothing is actually 3D. The "metal" is a lie painted by a fragment shader — a tiny function that runs once per pixel and decides what color that pixel should be. The trick has three parts: (1) a real physics formula for how metal reflects light at different angles (that's why gold looks warm head-on but shifts at a glance), (2) a fake "brushed grain" texture made from noise so it isn't mirror-smooth, and (3) a pretend environment of light blobs that the surface reflects. Tie those to the scroll angle and the card comes alive.

    Think of it as a stage magician's mirror: there's no real room behind it, just a clever painting that updates as you move.

    Physically-based metallic card rendering with Fresnel conductor reflections, environment mapping, normal-mapped micro-detail, and dynamic lighting — all driven by scroll gesture.

    How It Works

    Architecture

    ScrollView (horizontal)
  └─ Card[] (each has a Skia Canvas)
       └─ <Shader source={cardShader} uniforms={{ rotate, resolution, cardType }} />
    • scrollX → interpolated to rotation in range [-π, π]
    • Each card is also rotated in 3D via CSS transforms (perspective 1200, rotateY, base 18° tilt)
    • The shader renders the metallic surface; the card geometry is just a flat rect

    Uniforms

    Uniform Type Description
    rotate float Card rotation angle in radians (-π to π), from scroll offset
    resolution vec2 Card pixel dimensions
    cardType float Material selector: 0=Silver, 1=Gold, 2=Platinum, 3=Metal

    Core Shader Techniques

    1. Fresnel Conductor Equation (Real Metal Physics)

    This is NOT the cheap Schlick approximation. It uses the full Fresnel equations for conductors (metals), which have complex refractive indices (n + ik):

    vec3 fresnelConductor(float cosTheta, vec3 reflectivity, vec3 edgeTint) {
    vec3 r = clamp(reflectivity, vec3(0.0), vec3(0.99));
    vec3 r_sqrt = sqrt(r);
    // Derive n (refractive index) from reflectivity + edge tint
    vec3 n = mix((1.0 + r_sqrt) / (1.0 - r_sqrt), (1.0 - r) / (1.0 + r), edgeTint);
    // Derive k² (extinction coefficient) from n and reflectivity
    vec3 k2 = ((n + 1.0) * (n + 1.0) * r - (n - 1.0) * (n - 1.0)) / (1.0 - r);
    k2 = max(k2, vec3(0.0));
    vec3 k = sqrt(k2);
    // Full Fresnel: average of s-polarized and p-polarized reflectance
    vec3 rs_num = pow2(n) + pow2(k) - 2.0 * n * cosTheta + pow2(vec3(cosTheta));
    vec3 rs_den = pow2(n) + pow2(k) + 2.0 * n * cosTheta + pow2(vec3(cosTheta));
    vec3 rs = rs_num / rs_den;
    vec3 rp_num = (pow2(n) + pow2(k)) * pow2(vec3(cosTheta)) - 2.0 * n * cosTheta + vec3(1.0);
    vec3 rp_den = (pow2(n) + pow2(k)) * pow2(vec3(cosTheta)) + 2.0 * n * cosTheta + vec3(1.0);
    vec3 rp = rp_num / rp_den;
    return clamp(0.5 * (rs + rp), vec3(0.0), vec3(1.0));
}

    Why this matters: Real metals reflect different wavelengths at different angles. Gold looks yellow head-on but shifts warm at grazing angles. This equation captures that per-channel. Schlick approximation cannot.

    2. Perlin Noise Normal Mapping (Brushed Metal Grain)

    Generates a normal map procedurally from layered noise:

    float calculateMetallicDetail(vec2 uv, float cardType) {
    float fine   = noise(uv * 1600.0) * 0.35;   // fine grain
    float medium = noise(uv * 800.0)  * 0.3;     // medium grain
    float rough  = noise(uv * 400.0)  * 0.2;     // large grain
    // Directional grain (rotated 15°) for brushed look
    vec2 rotatedUV = vec2(
        uv.x * cos(PI * 0.15) - uv.y * sin(PI * 0.15),
        uv.x * sin(PI * 0.15) + uv.y * cos(PI * 0.15)
    );
    float directional = noise(rotatedUV * 1000.0) * 0.4;
    return (fine + medium + rough + directional) * 0.4 * intensity;
}

vec3 calculateNormal(vec2 uv, float normalStrength, float cardType) {
    vec2 eps = vec2(1.0) / resolution;
    float c = calculateMetallicDetail(uv, cardType);
    float r = calculateMetallicDetail(uv + vec2(eps.x, 0.0), cardType);
    float t = calculateMetallicDetail(uv + vec2(0.0, eps.y), cardType);
    return normalize(vec3(
        (r - c) * normalStrength * 2.0,
        (t - c) * normalStrength * 2.0,
        1.0
    ));
}

    Technique: Finite-difference normal map from height field. Sample noise at 3 points (center, right, up), compute derivatives → surface normal.

    3. Environment Mapping

    Fake environment map using spherical UV mapping + procedural light blobs:

    vec3 envMap(vec3 r, float roughness, float type) {
    vec2 envUV = vec2(atan(r.z, r.x) / (2.0 * PI) + 0.5, acos(r.y) / PI);
    // Two light positions in env map
    float intensity1 = pow(1.0 - distance(envUV, vec2(0.3, 0.4)) * 1.8, 6.0);
    float intensity2 = pow(1.0 - distance(envUV, vec2(0.7, 0.6)) * 1.8, 6.0);
    vec3 envColor = baseColor + highlight1 * intensity1 * 1.6 + highlight2 * intensity2 * 1.6;
    // Grazing angle color shift
    float colorShift = pow(1.0 - r.y, 4.0) * 0.8;
    return mix(envColor, mixColor, roughness);
}

    4. Dynamic Lighting

    Light position rotates with the card but reversed (so light appears fixed in world space):

    vec3 calculateDynamicLight(vec2 uv, float rotationAngle) {
    float c = cos(-rotationAngle);  // negative = light stays fixed
    float s = sin(-rotationAngle);
    vec3 rotatedLight = vec3(
        baseLight.x * c - baseLight.z * s,
        baseLight.y,
        baseLight.x * s + baseLight.z * c
    );
    return normalize(rotatedLight);
}

    5. Final Composition

    finalColor = mix(baseColor, envColor, fresnel)
           * diffuse lighting
           + specular highlights
           * metallic grain pattern
           + motion-based contrast
           + dynamic vignette
           + color shift from rotation

    Material Properties

    Property Silver Gold Platinum Metal
    Reflectivity 0.92, 0.92, 0.92 0.92, 0.88, 0.78 0.99, 0.98, 0.97 0.96, 0.96, 0.96
    Roughness 0.32 0.32 0.30 0.32
    Normal Strength 0.20 0.13 0.14 0.16
    Micro Detail 1.6 1.0 1.0 1.4

    Metal Shading Language Translation

    #include <metal_stdlib>
using namespace metal;

struct Uniforms {
    float rotate;
    float2 resolution;
    float cardType;
};

// --- Utility ---
float2 pow2(float2 x) { return x * x; }
float3 pow2(float3 x) { return x * x; }

float hash(float2 p) {
    float3 p3 = fract(float3(p.xyx) * float3(443.897, 441.423, 437.195));
    p3 += dot(p3, p3.yzx + 19.19);
    return fract((p3.x + p3.y) * p3.z);
}

float noise(float2 p) {
    float2 i = floor(p);
    float2 f = fract(p);
    f = f * f * (3.0 - 2.0 * f);
    float a = hash(i);
    float b = hash(i + float2(1.0, 0.0));
    float c = hash(i + float2(0.0, 1.0));
    float d = hash(i + float2(1.0, 1.0));
    return mix(mix(a, b, f.x), mix(c, d, f.x), f.y);
}

// --- Fresnel Conductor ---
float3 fresnelConductor(float cosTheta, float3 reflectivity, float3 edgeTint) {
    float3 r = clamp(reflectivity, float3(0.0), float3(0.99));
    float3 r_sqrt = sqrt(r);
    float3 n = mix((1.0 + r_sqrt) / (1.0 - r_sqrt),
                   (1.0 - r) / (1.0 + r), edgeTint);
    float3 k2 = ((n + 1.0) * (n + 1.0) * r - (n - 1.0) * (n - 1.0)) / (1.0 - r);
    k2 = max(k2, float3(0.0));
    float3 k = sqrt(k2);

    float ct2 = cosTheta * cosTheta;
    float3 n2k2 = n * n + k2;
    float3 rs = (n2k2 - 2.0 * n * cosTheta + ct2)
              / (n2k2 + 2.0 * n * cosTheta + ct2);
    float3 rp = (n2k2 * ct2 - 2.0 * n * cosTheta + 1.0)
              / (n2k2 * ct2 + 2.0 * n * cosTheta + 1.0);
    return clamp(0.5 * (rs + rp), float3(0.0), float3(1.0));
}

// --- Metallic Detail ---
float metallicDetail(float2 uv, float cardType, float2 res) {
    float fine   = noise(uv * 1600.0) * 0.35;
    float medium = noise(uv * 800.0)  * 0.3;
    float rough  = noise(uv * 400.0)  * 0.2;
    // Directional grain at 15°
    float angle = M_PI_F * 0.15;
    float2 rotUV = float2(uv.x * cos(angle) - uv.y * sin(angle),
                          uv.x * sin(angle) + uv.y * cos(angle));
    float dir = noise(rotUV * 1000.0) * 0.4;
    return (fine + medium + rough + dir) * 0.4;
}

float3 calcNormal(float2 uv, float strength, float cardType, float2 res) {
    float2 eps = 1.0 / res;
    float c = metallicDetail(uv, cardType, res);
    float r = metallicDetail(uv + float2(eps.x, 0), cardType, res);
    float t = metallicDetail(uv + float2(0, eps.y), cardType, res);
    return normalize(float3((r - c) * strength * 2.0,
                            (t - c) * strength * 2.0,
                            1.0));
}

// --- Fragment Shader (stitchable — drives .colorEffect) ---
// [[ stitchable ]] exposes this to SwiftUI's ShaderLibrary. The required
// (position, color) come first; every argument after them is a uniform
// you pass from Swift, in order. We ignore the incoming color and compute
// our own, so it's named inColor.

[[ stitchable ]] half4 cardShader(
    float2 position, half4 inColor,
    float rotate, float2 resolution, float cardType
) {
    float2 uv = position / resolution;
    float3 viewDir = normalize(float3(uv * 2.0 - 1.0, 1.0));

    // Material selection (simplified — expand per cardType)
    float3 reflectivity = float3(0.92); // silver default
    float3 edgeTint = float3(0.94);
    float3 baseColor = float3(0.15);
    float roughness = 0.32;

    // Dynamic light (rotates opposite to card)
    float c = cos(-rotate), s = sin(-rotate);
    float3 lightDir = normalize(float3(-3.0 * c - 2.5 * s, -0.8, -3.0 * s + 2.5 * c));

    // Normal from procedural grain
    float3 normal = calcNormal(uv, 0.2, cardType, resolution);

    // Fresnel
    float NdotV = max(dot(normal, viewDir), 0.0);
    float3 F = fresnelConductor(NdotV, reflectivity, edgeTint);

    // Environment reflection
    float3 reflectDir = reflect(-viewDir, normal);
    float2 envUV = float2(atan2(reflectDir.z, reflectDir.x) / (2.0 * M_PI_F) + 0.5,
                          acos(reflectDir.y) / M_PI_F);
    float i1 = pow(max(1.0 - distance(envUV, float2(0.3, 0.4)) * 1.8, 0.0), 6.0);
    float i2 = pow(max(1.0 - distance(envUV, float2(0.7, 0.6)) * 1.8, 0.0), 6.0);
    float3 envColor = baseColor + float3(0.9) * i1 * 1.6 + float3(0.85) * i2 * 1.6;

    // Specular
    float3 halfDir = normalize(lightDir + viewDir);
    float NdotH = max(dot(normal, halfDir), 0.0);
    float NdotL = max(dot(normal, lightDir), 0.0);
    float specPower = (1.0 - roughness) * 156.0 + 16.0;
    float spec = pow(NdotH, specPower);

    // Compose
    float3 color = mix(baseColor, envColor, F * 0.8);
    color = color * (0.5 + 0.7 * pow(NdotL, 1.4)) + baseColor * spec * 1.2;

    // Metallic grain
    float grain = metallicDetail(uv, cardType, resolution);
    color *= (1.0 + grain * 0.35);

    // Vignette
    float2 vc = float2(0.5 + sin(rotate) * 0.15, 0.5 + cos(rotate * 0.7) * 0.1);
    float vignette = 1.0 - length((uv - vc) * 1.5);
    color *= mix(0.85, 1.0, vignette);

    return half4(half3(color), 1.0h);
}

    SwiftUI Integration (iOS 17+)

    One [[ stitchable ]] function, one modifier — no MTKView, no renderer. The uniforms line up with the argument order in the shader:

    struct MetalCard: View {
    var rotation: Float
    var cardType: Float

    var body: some View {
        GeometryReader { geo in
            RoundedRectangle(cornerRadius: 24)
                .colorEffect(
                    ShaderLibrary.cardShader(
                        .float(rotation),        // rotate
                        .float2(geo.size),       // resolution
                        .float(cardType)         // cardType
                    )
                )
        }
    }
}

// Animate the tilt by driving `rotation` from a TimelineView or a drag gesture.

    You only reach for a hand-built MTKView/UIViewRepresentable render pass if you need something colorEffect can't express — multiple passes, vertex work, or feeding real geometry. For a per-pixel surface effect like this, stitchable is the whole job. See 17-metal-in-uikit for the full-pipeline path.

    The foundation

    • Fresnel conductor = the real-metal formula for how much light reflects at a given angle, computed per color channel so gold can shift warm at grazing angles.
    • Normal mapping = faking tiny surface bumps by sampling noise and measuring its slope, so flat geometry catches light like brushed metal.
    • Environment map = a pretend surrounding world (here, a couple of glowing light blobs) that the surface "reflects" instead of a real room.
    • Finite-difference normal = compute the bump direction by comparing a point to its right and up neighbors — slope = direction the surface faces.
    • Dynamic light = the light rotates opposite to the card so it appears pinned in place while the card turns.
    • Uniforms = the few values Swift hands the shader each frame (rotation, resolution, which metal) — think of them as the dials on the effect.
